### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q5.** Assume, counterfactually, that this case arose in a jurisdiction that applies the *Dingwall* exception, which relaxes the traditional strict physical imminence requirement for duress based on the psychological effects of systemic coercion. Assume Nietzsche had explicitly commanded Holmes to steal the money, had threatened Holmes for months, and Holmes reasonably believed police were unable to protect his mother. Under this framework, can Holmes satisfy the imminence requirement? (Fact 4, modified.)

(a) Yes, because the exception establishes that any pattern of continuous, sustained, and unpredictable threats creates a reasonable perception of imminent danger under the law, regardless of the prior relationship between the coercer and the defendant.
(b) No, because the exception that relaxes the strict physical imminence requirement is specifically tied to the dynamics of battering and intimate coercive control, which do not apply to a generalized extortion dispute between former business associates. <!-- correct -->
(c) Yes, because the exception eliminates the imminence requirement entirely in such cases, allowing any credible threat of future harm to satisfy the defense if the threatening party has a documented track record of severe violence against the defendant.
(d) No, because the exception strictly applies only when the defendant is physically confined, completely isolated, or actively restrained by the threatening party at the exact moment the underlying criminal offense is being committed by the defendant.
(e) No, because the exception is strictly limited to cases where the threatened physical harm is directed at the defendant personally, and it cannot be invoked based on generalized threats made against a family member or other third-party relation.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** Under *United States v. Dingwall*, the strict physical proximity and imminence requirements of duress are relaxed for defendants experiencing the effects of battering or coercive control. The specific psychological dynamics of battering can create a reasonable perception of imminent danger even when the coercer is physically absent. However, courts do not extend this exception to generalized criminal extortion or associate disputes, as doing so would swallow the traditional strict imminence rule. Thus, (b) is correct. Option (a) falsely broadens the doctrine into a blanket exception for any sustained threats. Option (c) falsely asserts the rule eliminates the imminence requirement entirely. Option (d) falsely claims physical confinement is required; *Dingwall* expressly held the batterer's physical presence or confinement is not necessary. Option (e) falsely states that the exception cannot be based on threats to third parties; traditional duress principles covering threats to close relatives still apply.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, duress-imminence-proximity, duress-corrupt-authorities], difficulty: medium, cognitive: analysis, trap: Students incorrectly apply Dingwall's relaxed imminence to general extortion cases rather than limiting it to battering/coercive control

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, duress-imminence-proximity refinement; *United States v. Dingwall*.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: If the jurisdiction has adopted the *Dingwall* exception focusing on the "psychological effects of systemic coercion," a student could argue that this rationale applies equitably to any relationship where systemic coercion exists. The underlying logic of the exception is that sustained trauma alters the defendant's reasonable perception of inescapable danger. Therefore, any continuous pattern of unpredictable threats that successfully instills this psychological effect should legally suffice to relax the imminence requirement, regardless of whether the coercer is an intimate partner or a business associate.
(b) Argument-for: *United States v. Dingwall* created a narrow exception to the strict physical imminence requirement specifically tailored to the psychological realities of battering and intimate partner coercive control. Courts are highly protective of the traditional imminence rule and have explicitly refused to extend the *Dingwall* rationale to general criminal extortion or gang disputes, fearing it would swallow the rule. Because the Holmes-Nietzsche dynamic is an extortion dispute between associates, the battering exception does not apply, and Holmes cannot satisfy the strict imminence requirement.
(c) Argument-for: A student could argue that by recognizing the "psychological effects of systemic coercion," the *Dingwall* exception functionally eliminates the imminence requirement as traditionally defined. If systemic threats and a documented track record of severe violence are enough to trigger the defense even when the coercer is absent, the concept of temporal "imminence" is effectively replaced by a broader inquiry into the credibility and inescapability of future harm. Thus, in cases of severe prolonged coercion, the imminence requirement is essentially discarded.
(d) Argument-for: A student might reason that even under a relaxed standard, duress requires an explanation for why the defendant could not safely seek police assistance. They could argue that the *Dingwall* exception only relaxes the coercer's physical presence if the defendant is otherwise physically isolated or confined by the coercer's network or resources. This creates a logical rule that the exception applies only when the defendant is actively restrained or physically completely isolated at the moment of the offense.
(e) Argument-for: A student could argue that the psychological evidence underlying the *Dingwall* exception (such as Battered Woman Syndrome) relates uniquely to the traumatized victim's hyper-vigilance regarding threats to their own bodily integrity. While traditional duress allows for threats to third parties, a student might reason that the specialized psychological relaxation of imminence cannot be automatically transferred to threats against a family member, making the exception strictly limited to self-directed threats.

Head-to-head: Option (b) correctly applies the specific, doctrinally bounded holding of *Dingwall*, which strictly anchors the relaxed imminence standard to the dynamics of battering and intimate coercive control rather than general extortion. All distractors are cleanly falsifiable due to their absolute language. Option (a) falsely claims the exception applies to "any pattern" "regardless of the prior relationship." Option (c) falsely claims the exception "eliminates the imminence requirement entirely." Option (d) imposes a false categorical requirement of physical confinement "at the exact moment." Option (e) imposes a false categorical ban on applying the defense to threats against family members.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "regardless of the prior relationship between the coercer and the defendant" — wrong because *Dingwall* specifically relies on the dynamics of battering/intimate partner violence and courts actively refuse to apply it to general extortion.
- (c): "eliminates the imminence requirement entirely" — wrong because *Dingwall* modifies or relaxes the imminence inquiry (assessing it through the lens of the psychological effects of battering) rather than eliminating the imminence element entirely.
- (d): "strictly applies only when the defendant is physically confined... at the exact moment" — wrong because *Dingwall* explicitly allows the defense even when the defendant is not physically confined or in the immediate physical presence of the coercer.
- (e): "strictly limited to cases where the threatened physical harm is directed at the defendant personally" — wrong because traditional duress extends to threats against close family members, and the *Dingwall* exception does not categorically abrogate this foundational rule.

Recommended fix: none
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q5.** Assume, counterfactually, that this case arose in a jurisdiction that applies the *Dingwall* exception, which relaxes the traditional strict physical imminence requirement for duress based on the psychological effects of systemic coercion. Assume Nietzsche had explicitly commanded Holmes to steal the money, had threatened Holmes for months, and Holmes reasonably believed police were unable to protect his mother. Under this framework, can Holmes satisfy the imminence requirement? (Fact 4, modified.)

(a) Yes, because the exception establishes that any pattern of continuous, sustained, and unpredictable threats creates a reasonable perception of imminent danger under the law, regardless of the prior relationship between the coercer and the defendant.
(b) No, because the exception that relaxes the strict physical imminence requirement is specifically tied to the dynamics of battering and intimate coercive control, which do not apply to a generalized extortion dispute between former business associates. <!-- correct -->
(c) Yes, because the exception eliminates the imminence requirement entirely in such cases, allowing any credible threat of future harm to satisfy the defense if the threatening party has a documented track record of severe violence against the defendant.
(d) No, because the exception strictly applies only when the defendant is physically confined, completely isolated, or actively restrained by the threatening party at the exact moment the underlying criminal offense is being committed by the defendant.
(e) No, because the exception is strictly limited to cases where the threatened physical harm is directed at the defendant personally, and it cannot be invoked based on generalized threats made against a family member or other third-party relation.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** Under *United States v. Dingwall*, the strict physical proximity and imminence requirements of duress are relaxed for defendants experiencing the effects of battering or coercive control. The specific psychological dynamics of battering can create a reasonable perception of imminent danger even when the coercer is physically absent. However, courts do not extend this exception to generalized criminal extortion or associate disputes, as doing so would swallow the traditional strict imminence rule. Thus, (b) is correct. Option (a) falsely broadens the doctrine into a blanket exception for any sustained threats. Option (c) falsely asserts the rule eliminates the imminence requirement entirely. Option (d) falsely claims physical confinement is required; *Dingwall* expressly held the batterer's physical presence or confinement is not necessary. Option (e) falsely states that the exception cannot be based on threats to third parties; traditional duress principles covering threats to close relatives still apply.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, duress-imminence-proximity, duress-corrupt-authorities], difficulty: medium, cognitive: analysis, trap: Students incorrectly apply Dingwall's relaxed imminence to general extortion cases rather than limiting it to battering/coercive control

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, duress-imminence-proximity refinement; *United States v. Dingwall*.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: If the jurisdiction has adopted the *Dingwall* exception focusing on the "psychological effects of systemic coercion," a student could argue that this rationale applies equitably to any relationship where systemic coercion exists. Therefore, any continuous pattern of unpredictable threats that successfully instills this psychological effect should legally suffice to relax the imminence requirement, regardless of whether the coercer is an intimate partner or a business associate.
(b) Argument-for: *United States v. ---

## Q06

### Grounding

**Q6.** Your supervisor asks you to analyze Markov's assault charge for attacking Wald under this jurisdiction's Model Penal Code (MPC) insanity framework. Markov acted under a schizophrenic delusion that Wald was an assassin sent to poison the shelter, genuinely believing his attack was morally justified to save lives. Markov later told police, "I was stopping an assassination." Dr. Cole will testify that while Markov understood he was physically striking a person, his acute psychotic episode prevented him from appreciating that his actions were wrong. Which of the following is the most accurate analysis of Markov's insanity defense?

(a) Markov has a strong insanity defense because he did not know the physical nature and quality of his act—he thought he was stopping an assassination, meaning he did not understand he was striking a person. This pure cognitive failure alone satisfies the MPC's threshold requirement for a full acquittal at trial.
(b) Markov does not have a viable insanity defense because his post-assault statement to police—"I was stopping an assassination"—demonstrates that he understood the physical mechanics of his actions, which automatically equates to appreciating wrongfulness and precludes lack of substantial capacity under the MPC.
(c) Markov does not have a viable insanity defense because paranoid schizophrenia is legally defined as an abnormality "manifested only by repeated criminal or otherwise antisocial conduct" and is therefore categorically excluded from qualifying as a mental disease under the explicit limiting provisions of the MPC.
(d) Markov has a strong insanity defense because, as a result of his schizophrenia (a qualifying mental disease), he lacked substantial capacity to appreciate the wrongfulness of his conduct—he understood he was physically striking a human being but genuinely believed the act was morally justified to save lives. <!-- correct -->
(e) Markov has a strong insanity defense because his schizophrenia entirely destroyed his capacity to control his physical behavior, satisfying the strict irresistible impulse test—he could not have stopped his attack, which represents the exact legal standard required to satisfy the MPC's volitional impairment rule.

**Answer:** (d)

**Explanation:** Under MPC § 4.01, a defendant is not responsible if, due to a mental disease or defect, he lacks "substantial capacity" to "appreciate" the wrongfulness of his conduct. Markov suffers from a qualifying mental disease (schizophrenia) and lacked substantial capacity to appreciate wrongfulness because his delusion led him to believe he was saving lives. (a) is incorrect because Markov understood the physical nature of his act (he knew he was striking a person). (b) is incorrect because understanding the physical mechanics of an act does not automatically equate to appreciating its wrongfulness. (c) is incorrect because schizophrenia is a paradigmatic mental disease; the MPC exclusion applies to psychopathy or antisocial personality disorder. (e) is incorrect because the MPC uses "lacks substantial capacity" rather than requiring complete destruction of control, and the facts establish a cognitive, rather than volitional, impairment.

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [insanity, mpc-appreciate-criminality, mnaghten-wrongness, qualifying-mental-disease], difficulty: easy, cognitive: application, trap: Students confuse the nature-and-quality prong with the wrongfulness prong

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, mnaghten-wrongness refinement; mpc-appreciate-criminality refinement; qualifying-mental-disease refinement.

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — MPC Insanity Defense, Ch 23, Refinement `mpc-appreciate-criminality`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q6.** Your supervisor asks you to analyze Markov's assault charge for attacking Wald under this jurisdiction's Model Penal Code (MPC) insanity framework. Markov acted under a schizophrenic delusion that Wald was an assassin sent to poison the shelter, genuinely believing his attack was morally justified to save lives. Markov later told police, "I was stopping an assassination." Dr. Cole will testify that while Markov understood he was physically striking a person, his acute psychotic episode prevented him from appreciating that his actions were wrong. Which of the following is the most accurate analysis of Markov's insanity defense?

(a) Markov has a strong insanity defense because he did not know the physical nature and quality of his act—he thought he was stopping an assassination, meaning he did not understand he was striking a person. This pure cognitive failure alone satisfies the MPC's threshold requirement for a full acquittal at trial.
(b) Markov does not have a viable insanity defense because his post-assault statement to police—"I was stopping an assassination"—demonstrates that he understood the physical mechanics of his actions, which automatically equates to appreciating wrongfulness and precludes lack of substantial capacity under the MPC.
(c) Markov does not have a viable insanity defense because paranoid schizophrenia is legally defined as an abnormality "manifested only by repeated criminal or otherwise antisocial conduct" and is therefore categorically excluded from qualifying as a mental disease under the explicit limiting provisions of the MPC.
(d) Markov has a strong insanity defense because, as a result of his schizophrenia (a qualifying mental disease), he lacked substantial capacity to appreciate the wrongfulness of his conduct—he understood he was physically striking a human being but genuinely believed the act was morally justified to save lives. <!-- correct -->
(e) Markov has a strong insanity defense because his schizophrenia entirely destroyed his capacity to control his physical behavior, satisfying the strict irresistible impulse test—he could not have stopped his attack, which represents the exact legal standard required to satisfy the MPC's volitional impairment rule.

**Answer:** (d)

**Explanation:** Under MPC § 4.01, a defendant is not responsible if, due to a mental disease or defect, he lacks "substantial capacity" to "appreciate" the wrongfulness of his conduct. Markov suffers from a qualifying mental disease (schizophrenia) and lacked substantial capacity to appreciate wrongfulness because his delusion led him to believe he was saving lives. (b) is incorrect because understanding the physical mechanics of an act does not automatically equate to appreciating its wrongfulness. (e) is incorrect because the MPC uses "lacks substantial capacity" rather than requiring complete destruction of control, and the facts establish a cognitive, rather than volitional, impairment.

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, mnaghten-wrongness refinement; mpc-appreciate-criminality refinement; qualifying-mental-disease refinement.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: Markov's delusion that Wald was an assassin fundamentally altered his perception of reality at the moment of the attack. Under the cognitive prong of the insanity defense, a profound misperception of reality might amount to not knowing the nature of the act. Because his physical act was inextricably linked to this delusion, a student could argue that he did not truly understand he was striking a human being, satisfying the threshold requirement for an acquittal.
(b) Argument-for: The MPC's insanity defense is tightly circumscribed to prevent defendants who understand the reality of their actions from escaping liability. Markov's statement "I was stopping an assassination" proves he understood the physical mechanics of his violence. One could argue that under strict interpretations, demonstrating an understanding of the mechanics of violence establishes an appreciation of reality that intrinsically precludes a finding of lacking substantial capacity. Thus, his post-assault statement dooms his defense.
(c) Argument-for: Under MPC § 4.01(2), the terms "mental disease or defect" explicitly exclude an abnormality manifested only by repeated criminal or otherwise antisocial conduct. A student could argue that because Markov's schizophrenia manifested in a violent, antisocial outbreak, it triggers this exclusion. Therefore, his condition functions legally like psychopathy in this context, making it categorically excluded under the MPC's limiting provisions.
(d) Argument-for: Under MPC § 4.01, a defendant is relieved of responsibility if, due to a mental disease or defect, he lacks substantial capacity to appreciate the criminality/wrongfulness of his conduct. The facts stipulate Markov has schizophrenia, a qualifying mental disease. Because his delusion caused him to genuinely believe his attack was morally justified to save lives, he lacked substantial capacity to appreciate the wrongfulness of his act. This perfectly maps onto the MPC's cognitive impairment standard.
(e) Argument-for: The MPC contains a volitional prong that excuses a defendant who lacks substantial capacity to conform his conduct to the requirements of the law. A student could argue that Markov's psychotic episode functionally operated as an irresistible impulse, destroying his capacity to control his behavior. If his control was entirely destroyed, it certainly meets the "lacks substantial capacity" threshold. Therefore, a student might reason that satisfying the strict irresistible impulse test inherently satisfies the MPC's volitional impairment rule.

Head-to-head: Option (d) correctly applies the MPC's standard of "lacks substantial capacity to appreciate wrongfulness" to a defendant who understood the physical act but suffered from a delusional moral justification. Option (a) is easily dismissed because it directly contradicts the prompt's stipulation that Markov "understood he was physically striking a person." Option (b) fails because it asserts an explicitly false legal absolute—that understanding mechanics "automatically equates" to appreciating wrongfulness. Option (c) introduces a blatantly false definitional claim, legally equating schizophrenia with the MPC's carve-out for psychopathy/antisocial personality disorder. Option (e) fails because it falsely claims the strict "irresistible impulse test" (requiring entire destruction of control) is the "exact legal standard" of the MPC's volitional prong (which only requires a lack of substantial capacity).

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "meaning he did not understand he was striking a person" — wrong because it contradicts the explicit prompt fact that "Markov understood he was physically striking a person." 
- (b): "automatically equates to appreciating wrongfulness and precludes lack of substantial capacity under the MPC" — wrong because knowing the physical mechanics of an act does not automatically mean a defendant appreciates its wrongfulness under the MPC.
- (c): "paranoid schizophrenia is legally defined as an abnormality 'manifested only by repeated criminal or otherwise antisocial conduct' and is therefore categorically excluded" — wrong because this MPC exclusion explicitly targets psychopathy and antisocial personality disorder, not paranoid schizophrenia.
- (e): "which represents the exact legal standard required to satisfy the MPC's volitional impairment rule" — wrong because the MPC standard is "lacks substantial capacity to conform his conduct," which was designed specifically to replace the strict irresistible impulse test's requirement of entire destruction of control.

Recommended fix: none

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q6.** Your supervisor asks you to analyze Markov's assault charge for attacking Wald under this jurisdiction's Model Penal Code (MPC) insanity framework. Markov acted under a schizophrenic delusion that Wald was an assassin sent to poison the shelter, genuinely believing his attack was morally justified to save lives. Markov later told police, "I was stopping an assassination." Dr. Cole will testify that while Markov understood he was physically striking a person, his acute psychotic episode prevented him from appreciating that his actions were wrong. Which of the following is the most accurate analysis of Markov's insanity defense?

(a) Markov has a strong insanity defense because he did not know the physical nature and quality of his act—he thought he was stopping an assassination, meaning he did not understand he was striking a person. This pure cognitive failure alone satisfies the MPC's threshold requirement for a full acquittal at trial.
(b) Markov does not have a viable insanity defense because his post-assault statement to police—"I was stopping an assassination"—demonstrates that he understood the physical mechanics of his actions, which automatically equates to appreciating wrongfulness and precludes lack of substantial capacity under the MPC.
(c) Markov does not have a viable insanity defense because paranoid schizophrenia is legally defined as an abnormality "manifested only by repeated criminal or otherwise antisocial conduct" and is therefore categorically excluded from qualifying as a mental disease under the explicit limiting provisions of the MPC.
(d) Markov has a strong insanity defense because, as a result of his schizophrenia (a qualifying mental disease), he lacked substantial capacity to appreciate the wrongfulness of his conduct—he understood he was physically striking a human being but genuinely believed the act was morally justified to save lives. <!-- correct -->
(e) Markov has a strong insanity defense because his schizophrenia entirely destroyed his capacity to control his physical behavior, satisfying the strict irresistible impulse test—he could not have stopped his attack, which represents the exact legal standard required to satisfy the MPC's volitional impairment rule.

**Answer:** (d)

**Explanation:** Under MPC § 4.01, a defendant is not responsible if, due to a mental disease or defect, he lacks "substantial capacity" to "appreciate" the wrongfulness of his conduct. Markov suffers from a qualifying mental disease (schizophrenia) and lacked substantial capacity to appreciate wrongfulness because his delusion led him to believe he was saving lives. (a) is incorrect because Markov understood the physical nature of his act (he knew he was striking a person). (b) is incorrect because understanding the physical mechanics of an act does not automatically equate to appreciating its wrongfulness. (c) is incorrect because schizophrenia is a paradigmatic mental disease; the MPC exclusion applies to psychopathy or antisocial personality disorder. (e) is incorrect because the MPC uses "lacks substantial capacity" rather than requiring complete destruction of control, and the facts establish a cognitive, rather than volitional, impairment.

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [insanity, mpc-appreciate-criminality, mnaghten-wrongness, qualifying-mental-disease], difficulty: easy, cognitive: application, trap: Students confuse the nature-and-quality prong with the wrongfulness prong

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, mnaghten-wrongness refinement; mpc-appreciate-criminality refinement; qualifying-mental-disease refinement.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student could argue that Markov's delusion fundamentally altered his perception of reality such that the conceptual "nature and quality" of the act was entirely transformed. By believing he was stopping a mass poisoning rather than committing an unprovoked assault, one could argue he failed to grasp the true physical nature of his conduct. Under this view, this extreme cognitive failure easily satisfies the insanity threshold.
(b) Argument-for: A student might argue that admitting to a deliberate, goal-oriented act ("stopping an assassination") proves Markov acted with conscious intent and factual awareness. Under a rigid interpretation of criminal responsibility, one might assume that knowing you are inflicting physical harm on someone to stop them demonstrates sufficient rational calculation that it negates any claim of lacking substantial capacity. 
(c) Argument-for: A student might recall the MPC's explicit caveat that "mental disease or defect" does not include abnormalities manifested by repeated criminal or antisocial conduct. If the student focuses solely on the violent nature of Markov's assault and his antisocial behavior, they could wrongly categorize his severe paranoia under this exclusion, arguing his condition falls entirely outside the MPC's protection.
(d) Argument-for: This directly and correctly applies the MPC § 4.01 standard. Markov suffers from schizophrenia, a recognized mental disease. While he understood the physical mechanics of his actions, his delusion that he was saving the shelter establishes that he lacked substantial capacity to appreciate the wrongfulness of his conduct, neatly satisfying the MPC's cognitive prong.
(e) Argument-for: A student could argue that a delusion so powerful it compels a preemptive attack effectively destroys the defendant's free will, fulfilling the volitional prong of the insanity defense. By characterizing his psychotic episode as an irresistible impulse that wholly overrode his behavioral control, a student could mistakenly conclude this perfectly matches the MPC requirement for volitional impairment.

Head-to-head: Option (d) correctly applies the MPC § 4.01 cognitive prong, properly distinguishing between understanding physical acts and appreciating wrongfulness. Option (a) directly contradicts the explicit facts of the prompt (which states he knew he was striking a person). Option (b) makes the false legal claim that understanding physical mechanics "automatically equates" to appreciating wrongfulness, collapsing the MPC's two distinct cognitive concepts. Option (c) falsely asserts that paranoid schizophrenia is legally defined as the psychopathy exclusion ("manifested only by repeated criminal... conduct"). Option (e) incorrectly states that the "strict irresistible impulse test" is the "exact legal standard" for the MPC's volitional prong, ignoring the MPC's "substantial capacity" standard. All distractors contain fully falsifiable errors. 

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "he did not understand he was striking a person" — wrong because it explicitly contradicts the prompt's factual premise that Markov "understood he was physically striking a person."
- (b): "automatically equates to appreciating wrongfulness" — wrong because the MPC specifically recognizes that a defendant can understand the physical mechanics of an act while still lacking substantial capacity to appreciate its wrongfulness.
- (c): "paranoid schizophrenia is legally defined as an abnormality 'manifested only by repeated criminal or otherwise antisocial conduct'" — wrong because this MPC exclusion explicitly targets psychopathy and antisocial personality disorder, not paradigmatic mental illnesses like schizophrenia.
- (e): "strict irresistible impulse test... which represents the exact legal standard required to satisfy the MPC's volitional impairment rule" — wrong because the MPC uses a "substantial capacity" standard, intentionally rejecting the "strict irresistible impulse" requirement of the complete destruction of control.

Recommended fix: none
-->

---

## Q07

### Grounding

**Q7.** Assume, counterfactually, that Markov's case arose in a jurisdiction that follows the federal Insanity Defense Reform Act (IDRA) standard (provided in the statutes) rather than the provided Model Penal Code (MPC) standard. Your supervisor asks how applying the IDRA rules instead of the MPC rules would change the analysis of Markov's attack on Wald (Fact 2). Which of the following is the most accurate comparison?

(a) The IDRA standard would make no difference because both the MPC and IDRA use identical cognitive tests—both ask whether the defendant "lacked substantial capacity to appreciate the wrongfulness" of the conduct. Given Markov's documented paranoid schizophrenia and his acute psychotic episode that severely impaired his reality testing, he clearly satisfies this "substantial capacity" threshold under either specific framework.
(b) The IDRA standard would defeat Markov's defense entirely because IDRA eliminates the wrongfulness prong and requires only that the defendant was unable to appreciate the "nature and quality" of the act. Since Markov understood the physical nature of his actions—that he was physically striking a person—his delusions about justification would be entirely irrelevant under IDRA's strictly limited, single-prong cognitive approach.
(c) The IDRA standard would make the defense easier because IDRA places the burden on the prosecution to disprove insanity beyond a reasonable doubt, whereas the MPC places the burden on the defendant. Therefore, if the jury harbored any reasonable doubt about whether Markov's paranoid schizophrenia impaired his cognition during the offense, they would be legally mandated to acquit him based on this failure of proof at trial.
(d) The IDRA standard would make the defense harder to establish because IDRA requires the disease be "severe," eliminates the volitional prong, replaces the MPC's "substantial capacity" with "unable to appreciate," and demands proof by clear and convincing evidence. However, Markov's documented paranoid schizophrenia with acute psychotic episodes causing command hallucinations would likely satisfy even this strict threshold. <!-- correct -->
(e) The IDRA standard would make the defense harder solely because IDRA adds the word "severe" to the mental disease requirement, which excludes psychotic disorders like paranoid schizophrenia unless the episode causes total physical incapacitation. Because Markov was not physically incapacitated, his delusions and command hallucinations would be deemed insufficient to establish an insanity defense under this federal framework.

**Answer:** (d)

**Explanation:** The provided IDRA statute differs from the MPC by requiring a "severe" mental disease, stating the defendant must be "unable" to appreciate wrongfulness (rather than lacking "substantial capacity"), omitting the volitional "conform his conduct" prong, and expressly placing the burden on the defendant by clear and convincing evidence. Markov's defense remains strong: paranoid schizophrenia with hallucinations is a paradigmatic severe disease, and his delusion that he is stopping an assassination shows he was unable to appreciate wrongfulness. (a) is incorrect because IDRA uses the stricter "unable" standard, not "substantial capacity." (b) is incorrect because the IDRA text explicitly retains the wrongfulness prong ("nature and quality or the wrongfulness"). (c) is incorrect because IDRA shifts the burden to the defendant by clear and convincing evidence, directly contradicting the claim that the prosecution must disprove it. (e) is incorrect because the statute does not require physical incapacitation.

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [insanity, federal-idra-requirements, burden-allocation-post-hinckley, mpc-appreciate-criminality], difficulty: medium, cognitive: analysis, trap: Students conflate IDRA and MPC because both have a cognitive prong

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, federal-idra-requirements refinement; burden-allocation-post-hinckley refinement.

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — federal IDRA requirements, Ch 23, Refinement `federal-idra-requirements` and `burden-allocation-post-hinckley`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q7.** Assume, counterfactually, that Markov's case arose in a jurisdiction that follows the federal Insanity Defense Reform Act (IDRA) standard (provided in the statutes) rather than the provided Model Penal Code (MPC) standard. Your supervisor asks how applying the IDRA rules instead of the MPC rules would change the analysis of Markov's attack on Wald (Fact 2). Which of the following is the most accurate comparison?

(a) The IDRA standard would make no difference because both the MPC and IDRA use identical cognitive tests—both ask whether the defendant "lacked substantial capacity to appreciate the wrongfulness" of the conduct. Given Markov's documented paranoid schizophrenia and his acute psychotic episode that severely impaired his reality testing, he clearly satisfies this "substantial capacity" threshold under either specific framework.
(b) The IDRA standard would defeat Markov's defense entirely because IDRA eliminates the wrongfulness prong and requires only that the defendant was unable to appreciate the "nature and quality" of the act. Since Markov understood the physical nature of his actions—that he was physically striking a person—his delusions about justification would be entirely irrelevant under IDRA's strictly limited, single-prong cognitive approach.
(c) The IDRA standard would make the defense easier because IDRA places the burden on the prosecution to disprove insanity beyond a reasonable doubt, whereas the MPC places the burden on the defendant. Therefore, if the jury harbored any reasonable doubt about whether Markov's paranoid schizophrenia impaired his cognition during the offense, they would be legally mandated to acquit him based on this failure of proof at trial.
(d) The IDRA standard would make the defense harder to establish because IDRA requires the disease be "severe," eliminates the volitional prong, replaces the MPC's "substantial capacity" with "unable to appreciate," and demands proof by clear and convincing evidence. However, Markov's documented paranoid schizophrenia with acute psychotic episodes causing command hallucinations would likely satisfy even this strict threshold. <!-- correct -->
(e) The IDRA standard would make the defense harder solely because IDRA adds the word "severe" to the mental disease requirement, which excludes psychotic disorders like paranoid schizophrenia unless the episode causes total physical incapacitation. Because Markov was not physically incapacitated, his delusions and command hallucinations would be deemed insufficient to establish an insanity defense under this federal framework.

**Answer:** (d)

**Explanation:** The provided IDRA statute differs from the MPC by requiring a "severe" mental disease, stating the defendant must be "unable" to appreciate wrongfulness (rather than lacking "substantial capacity"), omitting the volitional "conform his conduct" prong, and expressly placing the burden on the defendant by clear and convincing evidence. Markov's defense remains strong: paranoid schizophrenia with hallucinations is a paradigmatic severe disease, and his delusion that he is stopping an assassination shows he was unable to appreciate wrongfulness. (a) is incorrect because IDRA uses the stricter "unable" standard, not "substantial capacity." (b) is incorrect because the IDRA text explicitly retains the wrongfulness prong ("nature and quality or the wrongfulness"). (c) is incorrect because IDRA shifts the burden to the defendant by clear and convincing evidence, directly contradicting the claim that the prosecution must disprove it. (e) is incorrect because the statute does not require physical incapacitation.

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [insanity, federal-idra-requirements, burden-allocation-post-hinckley, mpc-appreciate-criminality], difficulty: medium, cognitive: analysis, trap: Students conflate IDRA and MPC because both have a cognitive prong

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, federal-idra-requirements refinement; burden-allocation-post-hinckley refinement.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student might argue that both the MPC and IDRA focus heavily on the cognitive prong, dealing with the defendant's understanding of their actions. Because Markov suffers from paranoid schizophrenia and an acute psychotic episode, his condition is so severe that the distinction between "lacking substantial capacity" and being "unable" to appreciate wrongfulness is a distinction without a difference in this specific context. Therefore, under this view, the standard applied would make no practical difference to the outcome of his case.
(b) Argument-for: A student could argue that IDRA's return to a M'Naghten-like standard was intended to strictly limit the defense to cases where the defendant literally did not know what they were physically doing. Under this theory, IDRA's text is interpreted to heavily prioritize the "nature and quality" aspect over a broader moral wrongfulness test. Thus, if Markov knew he was striking Wald, this strict reading would defeat his defense entirely regardless of his delusions.
(c) Argument-for: A student might recall that under traditional federal common law (such as the standard articulated in *Davis v. United States*), the burden of proof rested on the prosecution to prove the defendant's sanity beyond a reasonable doubt once the issue was raised. If a student mistakenly believes the IDRA codified rather than overturned this traditional federal rule, they would conclude that the federal standard makes the defense significantly easier than under the MPC, which often allows placing the burden on the defendant.
(d) Argument-for: This option correctly identifies every statutory difference between the MPC and the IDRA. The IDRA (18 U.S.C. § 17) requires a "severe" mental disease, explicitly removes the volitional ("conform conduct") prong, uses the stricter "unable" to appreciate language rather than "substantial capacity," and sets the burden of proof on the defendant by clear and convincing evidence. It also correctly notes that an acute episode of paranoid schizophrenia featuring command hallucinations still qualifies as a "severe" disease, meaning the defense remains viable although harder to establish.
(e) Argument-for: A student might recognize that Congress passed the IDRA to significantly narrow the insanity defense in response to the Hinckley acquittal. Taking this narrowing intent to its extreme, a student might infer that adding the word "severe" was meant to restrict the defense exclusively to defendants who were entirely incapacitated or acting in a state of sheer automatism. Under this strict but erroneous assumption, Markov's ability to physically act would disqualify him.

Head-to-head: Option (d) accurately states the law, citing the precise textual shifts in 18 U.S.C. § 17 relative to the MPC. The distractors are easily dismissed because they make blatant misstatements about the statutory text of IDRA. Option (a) directly quotes IDRA as using "substantial capacity," which is false. Option (b) claims IDRA removes the wrongfulness prong entirely, directly contradicting the statute's "nature and quality or the wrongfulness" language. Option (c) gets the burden of proof exactly backward, claiming IDRA places it on the prosecution. Option (e) invents a "total physical incapacitation" requirement that does not exist in the statute.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "both the MPC and IDRA use identical cognitive tests—both ask whether the defendant 'lacked substantial capacity to appreciate the wrongfulness'" — wrong because IDRA explicitly replaces "substantial capacity" with "unable to appreciate".
- (b): "IDRA eliminates the wrongfulness prong" — wrong because IDRA explicitly retains the phrase "nature and quality or the wrongfulness of his acts".
- (c): "IDRA places the burden on the prosecution to disprove insanity beyond a reasonable doubt" — wrong because IDRA affirmatively places the burden of proof on the defendant by clear and convincing evidence.
- (e): "which excludes psychotic disorders like paranoid schizophrenia unless the episode causes total physical incapacitation" — wrong because IDRA has no physical incapacitation requirement, and paranoid schizophrenia is actually the paradigmatic "severe mental disease" intended to be covered by the defense.

Recommended fix: none
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q7.** Assume, counterfactually, that Markov's case arose in a jurisdiction that follows the federal Insanity Defense Reform Act (IDRA) standard (provided in the statutes) rather than the provided Model Penal Code (MPC) standard. Your supervisor asks how applying the IDRA rules instead of the MPC rules would change the analysis of Markov's attack on Wald (Fact 2). Which of the following is the most accurate comparison?

(a) The IDRA standard would make no difference because both the MPC and IDRA use identical cognitive tests—both ask whether the defendant "lacked substantial capacity to appreciate the wrongfulness" of the conduct. Given Markov's documented paranoid schizophrenia and his acute psychotic episode that severely impaired his reality testing, he clearly satisfies this "substantial capacity" threshold under either specific framework.
(b) The IDRA standard would defeat Markov's defense entirely because IDRA eliminates the wrongfulness prong and requires only that the defendant was unable to appreciate the "nature and quality" of the act. Since Markov understood the physical nature of his actions—that he was physically striking a person—his delusions about justification would be entirely irrelevant under IDRA's strictly limited, single-prong cognitive approach.
(c) The IDRA standard would make the defense easier because IDRA places the burden on the prosecution to disprove insanity beyond a reasonable doubt, whereas the MPC places the burden on the defendant. Therefore, if the jury harbored any reasonable doubt about whether Markov's paranoid schizophrenia impaired his cognition during the offense, they would be legally mandated to acquit him based on this failure of proof at trial.
(d) The IDRA standard would make the defense harder to establish because IDRA requires the disease be "severe," eliminates the volitional prong, replaces the MPC's "substantial capacity" with "unable to appreciate," and demands proof by clear and convincing evidence. However, Markov's documented paranoid schizophrenia with acute psychotic episodes causing command hallucinations would likely satisfy even this strict threshold. <!-- correct -->
(e) The IDRA standard would make the defense harder solely because IDRA adds the word "severe" to the mental disease requirement, which excludes psychotic disorders like paranoid schizophrenia unless the episode causes total physical incapacitation. Because Markov was not physically incapacitated, his delusions and command hallucinations would be deemed insufficient to establish an insanity defense under this federal framework.

**Answer:** (d)

**Explanation:** The provided IDRA statute differs from the MPC by requiring a "severe" mental disease, stating the defendant must be "unable" to appreciate wrongfulness (rather than lacking "substantial capacity"), omitting the volitional "conform his conduct" prong, and expressly placing the burden on the defendant by clear and convincing evidence. Markov's defense remains strong: paranoid schizophrenia with hallucinations is a paradigmatic severe disease, and his delusion that he is stopping an assassination shows he was unable to appreciate wrongfulness. (a) is incorrect because IDRA uses the stricter "unable" standard, not "substantial capacity." (b) is incorrect because the IDRA text explicitly retains the wrongfulness prong ("nature and quality or the wrongfulness"). (c) is incorrect because IDRA shifts the burden to the defendant by clear and convincing evidence, directly contradicting the claim that the prosecution must disprove it. (e) is incorrect because the statute does not require physical incapacitation.

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [insanity, federal-idra-requirements, burden-allocation-post-hinckley, mpc-appreciate-criminality], difficulty: medium, cognitive: analysis, trap: Students conflate IDRA and MPC because both have a cognitive prong

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, federal-idra-requirements refinement; burden-allocation-post-hinckley refinement.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student might argue that both the MPC and IDRA are fundamentally cognitive tests in the context of wrongfulness. Under both, a defendant like Markov who suffers a severe delusion fails the test. Therefore, the difference in phrasing between "lacked substantial capacity" and "unable" is a semantic distinction that functionally operates identically in the face of a total psychotic break, meaning it would make no difference to the ultimate outcome.
(b) Argument-for: One could argue that IDRA's strictest interpretation limits insanity to M'Naghten's "nature and quality" prong, stripping away broader MPC concepts. If a student recalls that IDRA was a conservative reaction to the Hinckley acquittal, they might logically, albeit incorrectly, conclude it completely eliminated the "wrongfulness" prong to prevent acquittals based on moral delusions. Since Markov understood he was physically striking someone, this strict M'Naghten-style reading would defeat his defense.
(c) Argument-for: Historically, federal law (under *Davis v. United States*) required the prosecution to prove sanity beyond a reasonable doubt. A student might assert that this historical federal standard still reflects the baseline "federal standard," and thus applying it would make the defense much easier. Under this view, IDRA simply codified the federal cognitive focus while maintaining the government's traditional, heavy burden of proof.
(d) Argument-for: This option perfectly synthesizes the statutory changes enacted by the IDRA: the addition of "severe," the shift from "substantial capacity" to "unable," the elimination of the volitional prong, and the burden shift to the defendant by clear and convincing evidence. It correctly concludes that while the standard is strictly harder, a defendant experiencing acute psychotic hallucinations and delusions regarding justification (like Markov) would still likely meet the threshold for being unable to appreciate the wrongfulness of his acts.
(e) Argument-for: A student could argue that the IDRA's addition of "severe" to mental disease was intended to categorically exclude anything short of complete physical incapacitation. Under this theory, Congress intended to eliminate all defenses for ambulatory defendants who can physically execute a coordinated crime. Therefore, because Markov was not physically paralyzed or catatonic, he fails the "severity" requirement.

Head-to-head: 
Options (a), (b), (c), and (e) each rely on explicit legal misstatements about the text or function of the IDRA. Option (a) incorrectly claims IDRA uses the exact phrase "lacked substantial capacity." Option (b) incorrectly claims IDRA eliminates the "wrongfulness" prong. Option (c) incorrectly claims IDRA places the burden on the prosecution. Option (e) incorrectly claims IDRA requires "total physical incapacitation." These are highly falsifiable propositions that outright contradict the plain text of 18 U.S.C. § 17. Option (d) correctly identifies every statutory change enacted by the IDRA and provides a sound legal conclusion regarding Markov's likelihood of success. Thus, (d) is unambiguously the correct answer.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "both ask whether the defendant 'lacked substantial capacity to appreciate the wrongfulness' of the conduct." — wrong because IDRA replaces "lacked substantial capacity" with "unable to appreciate".
- (b): "IDRA eliminates the wrongfulness prong" — wrong because 18 U.S.C. § 17 explicitly states "unable to appreciate the nature and quality or the wrongfulness of his acts."
- (c): "IDRA places the burden on the prosecution to disprove insanity beyond a reasonable doubt" — wrong because IDRA explicitly shifted the burden to the defendant to prove insanity by clear and convincing evidence.
- (e): "excludes psychotic disorders like paranoid schizophrenia unless the episode causes total physical incapacitation." — wrong because the IDRA does not contain any "total physical incapacitation" requirement, and paranoid schizophrenia is a paradigmatic severe mental disease.

Recommended fix: none
-->

## Q08

### Grounding

**Q8.** Assume, counterfactually, that Markov's case arose in a jurisdiction that has abolished the insanity defense and follows the mens rea model (like Kansas). The jurisdiction's assault statute requires "purposely or knowingly causing bodily injury to another person." How would this change Markov's defense options? (Fact 2.)

(a) Markov would have no defense options because abolition states treat mental illness as entirely irrelevant to criminal proceedings; therefore, psychiatric records and expert testimony are strictly inadmissible for any purpose, even if the defendant lacked the ability to form intent.
(b) Markov could still raise an insanity defense because the Supreme Court held in *Kahler v. Kansas* that the Due Process Clause requires every state to provide at least a M'Naghten-style wrongfulness defense to ensure mentally ill defendants are not unjustly punished for their acts.
(c) Markov's mental illness could only be used to negate the mens rea element of assault; however, because his delusion about stopping an assassin confirms he acted purposely or knowingly to cause bodily injury, his psychiatric evidence regarding wrongfulness would be irrelevant.
(d) Markov would be acquitted because the mens rea model permits a complete affirmative defense whenever the defendant's severe mental illness caused a sincere but delusional belief that the underlying physical conduct was legally justified to protect others from an imminent harm.
(e) Markov could use his mental illness both to negate mens rea and to argue he did not know his actions were wrong, because abolition states permit the admission of such psychiatric evidence for all purposes bearing on criminal responsibility and moral culpability at the trial stage.

**Answer:** (c)

**Explanation:** Under the mens rea model (*Kahler v. Kansas*), the traditional insanity defense is abolished, and mental illness evidence is admissible only to negate the required statutory mens rea. Markov understood he was striking a person to stop an assassination, meaning he acted purposely or knowingly to cause bodily injury. Therefore, his mental illness evidence only bears on his inability to appreciate wrongfulness, which is irrelevant and inadmissible under this model. Option (a) is false because abolition states permit mental disease evidence to negate mens rea; they do not entirely exclude it. Option (b) is false because *Kahler* held the Due Process Clause does not require states to provide a wrongfulness-based insanity defense. Option (d) is false because the mens rea model provides no freestanding justification defense based on a delusional belief. Option (e) is false because abolition states restrict mental illness evidence strictly to the mens rea inquiry, explicitly excluding evidence bearing solely on moral wrongfulness.

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [insanity, mens-rea-model-abolition, kahler-constitutional-floor], difficulty: medium, cognitive: analysis, trap: Students assume abolition states provide no protection at all, or that a delusional motive negates intent to cause physical harm

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, mens-rea-model-abolition refinement; *Kahler v. Kansas*; radue-admissibility-gatekeeping refinement.

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — Mens Rea Model Abolition / Kahler v. Kansas, Ch 23, Refinements `mens-rea-model-abolition` and `kahler-constitutional-floor`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Ambiguity Audit

**Q8.** Assume, counterfactually, that Markov's case arose in a jurisdiction that has abolished the insanity defense and follows the mens rea model (like Kansas). The jurisdiction's assault statute requires "purposely or knowingly causing bodily injury to another person." How would this change Markov's defense options? (Fact 2.)

(a) Markov would have no defense options because abolition states treat mental illness as entirely irrelevant to criminal proceedings; therefore, psychiatric records and expert testimony are strictly inadmissible for any purpose, even if the defendant lacked the ability to form intent.
(b) Markov could still raise an insanity defense because the Supreme Court held in *Kahler v. Kansas* that the Due Process Clause requires every state to provide at least a M'Naghten-style wrongfulness defense to ensure mentally ill defendants are not unjustly punished for their acts.
(c) Markov's mental illness could only be used to negate the mens rea element of assault; however, because his delusion about stopping an assassin confirms he acted purposely or knowingly to cause bodily injury, his psychiatric evidence regarding wrongfulness would be irrelevant.
(d) Markov would be acquitted because the mens rea model permits a complete affirmative defense whenever the defendant's severe mental illness caused a sincere but delusional belief that the underlying physical conduct was legally justified to protect others from an imminent harm.
(e) Markov could use his mental illness both to negate mens rea and to argue he did not know his actions were wrong, because abolition states permit the admission of such psychiatric evidence for all purposes bearing on criminal responsibility and moral culpability at the trial stage.

**Answer:** (c)

**Explanation:** Under the mens rea model (*Kahler v. Kansas*), the traditional insanity defense is abolished, and mental illness evidence is admissible only to negate the required statutory mens rea. Markov understood he was striking a person to stop an assassination, meaning he acted purposely or knowingly to cause bodily injury. Therefore, his mental illness evidence only bears on his inability to appreciate wrongfulness, which is irrelevant and inadmissible under this model. Option (a) is false because abolition states permit mental disease evidence to negate mens rea; they do not entirely exclude it. Option (b) is false because *Kahler* held the Due Process Clause does not require states to provide a wrongfulness-based insanity defense. Option (d) is false because the mens rea model provides no freestanding justification defense based on a delusional belief. Option (e) is false because abolition states restrict mental illness evidence strictly to the mens rea inquiry, explicitly excluding evidence bearing solely on moral wrongfulness.

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [insanity, mens-rea-model-abolition, kahler-constitutional-floor], difficulty: medium, cognitive: analysis, trap: Students assume abolition states provide no protection at all, or that a delusional motive negates intent to cause physical harm

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, mens-rea-model-abolition refinement; *Kahler v. Kansas*; radue-admissibility-gatekeeping refinement.

### Edge Case Audit

**Q8.** Assume, counterfactually, that Markov's case arose in a jurisdiction that has abolished the insanity defense and follows the mens rea model (like Kansas). The jurisdiction's assault statute requires "purposely or knowingly causing bodily injury to another person." How would this change Markov's defense options? (Fact 2.)

(a) Markov would have no defense options because abolition states treat mental illness as entirely irrelevant to criminal proceedings; therefore, psychiatric records and expert testimony are strictly inadmissible for any purpose, even if the defendant lacked the ability to form intent.
(b) Markov could still raise an insanity defense because the Supreme Court held in *Kahler v. Kansas* that the Due Process Clause requires every state to provide at least a M'Naghten-style wrongfulness defense to ensure mentally ill defendants are not unjustly punished for their acts.
(c) Markov's mental illness could only be used to negate the mens rea element of assault; however, because his delusion about stopping an assassin confirms he acted purposely or knowingly to cause bodily injury, his psychiatric evidence regarding wrongfulness would be irrelevant.
(d) Markov would be acquitted because the mens rea model permits a complete affirmative defense whenever the defendant's severe mental illness caused a sincere but delusional belief that the underlying physical conduct was legally justified to protect others from an imminent harm.
(e) Markov could use his mental illness both to negate mens rea and to argue he did not know his actions were wrong, because abolition states permit the admission of such psychiatric evidence for all purposes bearing on criminal responsibility and moral culpability at the trial stage.

**Answer:** (c)

**Explanation:** Under the mens rea model (*Kahler v. Kansas*), the traditional insanity defense is abolished, and mental illness evidence is admissible only to negate the required statutory mens rea. Markov understood he was striking a person to stop an assassination, meaning he acted purposely or knowingly to cause bodily injury. Therefore, his mental illness evidence only bears on his inability to appreciate wrongfulness, which is irrelevant and inadmissible under this model. Option (a) is false because abolition states permit mental disease evidence to negate mens rea; they do not entirely exclude it. Option (b) is false because *Kahler* held the Due Process Clause does not require states to provide a wrongfulness-based insanity defense. Option (d) is false because the mens rea model provides no freestanding justification defense based on a delusional belief. Option (e) is false because abolition states restrict mental illness evidence strictly to the mens rea inquiry, explicitly excluding evidence bearing solely on moral wrongfulness.

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [insanity, mens-rea-model-abolition, kahler-constitutional-floor], difficulty: medium, cognitive: analysis, trap: Students assume abolition states provide no protection at all, or that a delusional motive negates intent to cause physical harm

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, mens-rea-model-abolition refinement; *Kahler v. Kansas*; radue-admissibility-gatekeeping refinement.

1. Fact Pattern Booby Traps: pass
2. Cross-Doctrine Clashes: pass
3. Cross-Question Spoilers: pass
Recommended fix: n/a
-->

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q8.** Assume, counterfactually, that Markov's case arose in a jurisdiction that has abolished the insanity defense and follows the mens rea model (like Kansas). The jurisdiction's assault statute requires "purposely or knowingly causing bodily injury to another person." How would this change Markov's defense options? (Fact 2.)

(a) Markov would have no defense options because abolition states treat mental illness as entirely irrelevant to criminal proceedings; therefore, psychiatric records and expert testimony are strictly inadmissible for any purpose, even if the defendant lacked the ability to form intent.
(b) Markov could still raise an insanity defense because the Supreme Court held in *Kahler v. Kansas* that the Due Process Clause requires every state to provide at least a M'Naghten-style wrongfulness defense to ensure mentally ill defendants are not unjustly punished for their acts.
(c) Markov's mental illness could only be used to negate the mens rea element of assault; however, because his delusion about stopping an assassin confirms he acted purposely or knowingly to cause bodily injury, his psychiatric evidence regarding wrongfulness would be irrelevant.
(d) Markov would be acquitted because the mens rea model permits a complete affirmative defense whenever the defendant's severe mental illness caused a sincere but delusional belief that the underlying physical conduct was legally justified to protect others from an imminent harm.
(e) Markov could use his mental illness both to negate mens rea and to argue he did not know his actions were wrong, because abolition states permit the admission of such psychiatric evidence for all purposes bearing on criminal responsibility and moral culpability at the trial stage.

**Answer:** (c)

**Explanation:** Under the mens rea model (*Kahler v. Kansas*), the traditional insanity defense is abolished, and mental illness evidence is admissible only to negate the required statutory mens rea. Markov understood he was striking a person to stop an assassination, meaning he acted purposely or knowingly to cause bodily injury. Therefore, his mental illness evidence only bears on his inability to appreciate wrongfulness, which is irrelevant and inadmissible under this model. Option (a) is false because abolition states permit mental disease evidence to negate mens rea; they do not entirely exclude it. Option (b) is false because *Kahler* held the Due Process Clause does not require states to provide a wrongfulness-based insanity defense. Option (d) is false because the mens rea model provides no freestanding justification defense based on a delusional belief. Option (e) is false because abolition states restrict mental illness evidence strictly to the mens rea inquiry, explicitly excluding evidence bearing solely on moral wrongfulness.

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [insanity, mens-rea-model-abolition, kahler-constitutional-floor], difficulty: medium, cognitive: analysis, trap: Students assume abolition states provide no protection at all, or that a delusional motive negates intent to cause physical harm

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, mens-rea-model-abolition refinement; *Kahler v. Kansas*; radue-admissibility-gatekeeping refinement.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student might reason that if a state has "abolished the insanity defense," it reflects a legislative judgment that mental illness has no place in assessing guilt. Thus, courts in those jurisdictions would logically exclude all psychiatric evidence to prevent the defense from sneaking insanity concepts through the back door.
(b) Argument-for: A student might incorrectly recall the outcome of *Kahler v. Kansas*, confusing the petitioner's argument with the Court's holding. Since the Eighth Amendment prohibits cruel and unusual punishment, one could persuasively but incorrectly argue the Supreme Court established a constitutional baseline requiring states to assess a defendant's capacity to recognize wrongfulness.
(c) Argument-for: This is the correct application of the mens rea model validated in *Kahler v. Kansas*. Since Markov intended the physical act of causing bodily injury to another person, he fulfilled the statutory mens rea of "purposely or knowingly." Because his delusion primarily impacts his understanding of whether the act was morally or legally wrong—an inquiry precluded by the abolition of the insanity defense—his evidence is inadmissible to defeat the charge.
(d) Argument-for: A student might conflate the mens rea model with a subjective justification defense. If Markov sincerely believed he was stopping an assassin, a student could argue he lacked the true "criminal intent" necessary for assault, reasoning that the mens rea model inherently accommodates delusional self-defense or defense of others.
(e) Argument-for: A student might assume that "abolishing the insanity defense" simply means downgrading it from an affirmative defense to an evidentiary rule where all psychiatric evidence is weighed by the jury to assess general moral culpability. Under this view, abolition merely changes the procedural mechanism, not the substantive relevance of wrongfulness.

Head-to-head: 
Option (c) accurately synthesizes the rule from *Kahler v. Kansas*: in a mens rea model jurisdiction, psychiatric evidence is only admissible if it defeats the specific mental state required by the statute, which a delusion of justification does not do. The distractors are decisively flawed. Option (a) falsely claims an absolute evidentiary bar. Option (b) legally reverses the actual holding of *Kahler*. Option (d) fabricates a complete affirmative defense under the mens rea model. Option (e) contradicts the core definition of an abolition jurisdiction by claiming moral wrongfulness evidence remains admissible. 

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "psychiatric records and expert testimony are strictly inadmissible for any purpose" — wrong because mens rea model states explicitly allow this evidence to negate the statutory mens rea.
- (b): "the Supreme Court held in Kahler v. Kansas that the Due Process Clause requires every state to provide at least a M'Naghten-style wrongfulness defense" — wrong because *Kahler* held the exact opposite, ruling that Due Process does not mandate a wrongfulness test.
- (d): "the mens rea model permits a complete affirmative defense whenever the defendant's severe mental illness caused a sincere but delusional belief" — wrong because the mens rea model provides no such affirmative defense and solely limits the inquiry to the elements of the offense.
- (e): "abolition states permit the admission of such psychiatric evidence for all purposes bearing on criminal responsibility and moral culpability" — wrong because abolition states expressly prohibit evidence admitted solely to show an inability to appreciate moral wrongfulness.

Recommended fix: none
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q8.** Assume, counterfactually, that Markov's case arose in a jurisdiction that has abolished the insanity defense and follows the mens rea model (like Kansas). The jurisdiction's assault statute requires "purposely or knowingly causing bodily injury to another person." How would this change Markov's defense options? (Fact 2.)

(a) Markov would have no defense options because abolition states treat mental illness as entirely irrelevant to criminal proceedings; therefore, psychiatric records and expert testimony are strictly inadmissible for any purpose, even if the defendant lacked the ability to form intent.
(b) Markov could still raise an insanity defense because the Supreme Court held in *Kahler v. Kansas* that the Due Process Clause requires every state to provide at least a M'Naghten-style wrongfulness defense to ensure mentally ill defendants are not unjustly punished for their acts.
(c) Markov's mental illness could only be used to negate the mens rea element of assault; however, because his delusion about stopping an assassin confirms he acted purposely or knowingly to cause bodily injury, his psychiatric evidence regarding wrongfulness would be irrelevant.
(d) Markov would be acquitted because the mens rea model permits a complete affirmative defense whenever the defendant's severe mental illness caused a sincere but delusional belief that the underlying physical conduct was legally justified to protect others from an imminent harm.
(e) Markov could use his mental illness both to negate mens rea and to argue he did not know his actions were wrong, because abolition states permit the admission of such psychiatric evidence for all purposes bearing on criminal responsibility and moral culpability at the trial stage.

**Answer:** (c)

**Explanation:** Under the mens rea model (*Kahler v. Kansas*), the traditional insanity defense is abolished, and mental illness evidence is admissible only to negate the required statutory mens rea. Markov understood he was striking a person to stop an assassination, meaning he acted purposely or knowingly to cause bodily injury. Therefore, his mental illness evidence only bears on his inability to appreciate wrongfulness, which is irrelevant and inadmissible under this model. Option (a) is false because abolition states permit mental disease evidence to negate mens rea; they do not entirely exclude it. Option (b) is false because *Kahler* held the Due Process Clause does not require states to provide a wrongfulness-based insanity defense. Option (d) is false because the mens rea model provides no freestanding justification defense based on a delusional belief. Option (e) is false because abolition states restrict mental illness evidence strictly to the mens rea inquiry, explicitly excluding evidence bearing solely on moral wrongfulness.

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [insanity, mens-rea-model-abolition, kahler-constitutional-floor], difficulty: medium, cognitive: analysis, trap: Students assume abolition states provide no protection at all, or that a delusional motive negates intent to cause physical harm

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, mens-rea-model-abolition refinement; *Kahler v. Kansas*; radue-admissibility-gatekeeping refinement.

## Q09

### Grounding

**Q9.** Your supervisor asks a question about Markov's co-occurring diagnoses: Assume that the prosecution argues Markov's antisocial personality disorder -- not his schizophrenia -- drove his violent behavior. Under the MPC insanity framework that this jurisdiction follows (Facts 2 and 6), how should the defense respond?

(a) The defense should concede that the co-occurring antisocial personality disorder defeats the insanity defense because the MPC excludes any defendant who has been diagnosed with a personality disorder from raising the defense, regardless of whether they also have a qualifying mental illness. Under this view, the mere presence of a personality disorder acts as an absolute bar to any insanity claim.
(b) The defense should argue that both diagnoses should be treated as a single qualifying mental illness because the MPC provides that co-occurring disorders automatically merge into one legal category when they appear together in the same patient. This supposed merger doctrine prevents the prosecution from attempting to parse the relative causal contributions of the distinct psychiatric conditions.
(c) The defense should argue that the personality disorder exclusion does not apply because Markov's antisocial personality disorder is accompanied by schizophrenia, and any co-occurring psychotic disorder automatically satisfies the causation element for all purposes, making the prosecution's argument about which specific disorder actually "drove" the violent behavior completely legally irrelevant.
(d) The defense should argue that antisocial personality disorder standing alone cannot qualify as a "mental disease or defect" under MPC Section 4.01(2), but the exclusion applies only to the threshold disease requirement. Therefore, if the qualifying disease (schizophrenia) caused the incapacity, the defense legally survives even if the personality disorder also contributed to Markov's behavior. <!-- correct -->
(e) The defense should concede that the co-occurring personality disorder creates an insurmountable causation problem because the prosecution need only show that the personality disorder was a but-for cause of the conduct to defeat the defense entirely, regardless of the qualifying disease's role. Under this standard, any causal contribution from the personality disorder precludes a valid insanity plea.

**Answer:** (d)

**Explanation:** Under MPC § 4.01(2), an abnormality manifested "only by repeated criminal or otherwise anti-social conduct" does not qualify as a mental disease. The word "only" does the critical work: a personality disorder standing alone cannot qualify, but it does not disqualify a defendant with a co-occurring mental illness. The exclusion applies to the threshold disease requirement, not causation. If the qualifying disease (schizophrenia) caused the incapacity, the defense survives even if the personality disorder contributed. (a) is incorrect because the MPC excludes abnormalities manifested *only* by antisocial conduct; it is not an absolute bar for co-occurring conditions. (b) is incorrect because the MPC contains no "automatic merger" doctrine; the disorders remain legally distinct for the causation inquiry. (c) is incorrect because a co-occurring psychotic disorder does not automatically satisfy the causation element; the defense must still prove the qualifying disease caused the incapacity. (e) is incorrect because the statute does not require the qualifying disease to be the sole cause, nor does any contribution from a personality disorder defeat the defense entirely if the qualifying disease caused the requisite incapacity.

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [insanity, personality-disorder-exclusion, co-occurring-causation, qualifying-mental-disease], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis, trap: Students treat the personality disorder exclusion as a taint rule

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, personality-disorder-exclusion refinement; co-occurring-causation refinement; *State v. Meiser*.

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — personality disorder exclusion / co-occurring causation, Ch 23, Refinement `personality-disorder-exclusion` and `co-occurring-causation`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->
