### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q9.** Your supervisor asks a question about Markov's co-occurring diagnoses: Assume that the prosecution argues Markov's antisocial personality disorder -- not his schizophrenia -- drove his violent behavior. Under the MPC insanity framework that this jurisdiction follows (Facts 2 and 6), how should the defense respond?

(a) The defense should concede that the co-occurring antisocial personality disorder defeats the insanity defense because the MPC excludes any defendant who has been diagnosed with a personality disorder from raising the defense, regardless of whether they also have a qualifying mental illness. Under this view, the mere presence of a personality disorder acts as an absolute bar to any insanity claim.
(b) The defense should argue that both diagnoses should be treated as a single qualifying mental illness because the MPC provides that co-occurring disorders automatically merge into one legal category when they appear together in the same patient. This supposed merger doctrine prevents the prosecution from attempting to parse the relative causal contributions of the distinct psychiatric conditions.
(c) The defense should argue that the personality disorder exclusion does not apply because Markov's antisocial personality disorder is accompanied by schizophrenia, and any co-occurring psychotic disorder automatically satisfies the causation element for all purposes, making the prosecution's argument about which specific disorder actually "drove" the violent behavior completely legally irrelevant.
(d) The defense should argue that antisocial personality disorder standing alone cannot qualify as a "mental disease or defect" under MPC Section 4.01(2), but the exclusion applies only to the threshold disease requirement. Therefore, if the qualifying disease (schizophrenia) caused the incapacity, the defense legally survives even if the personality disorder also contributed to Markov's behavior. <!-- correct -->
(e) The defense should concede that the co-occurring personality disorder creates an insurmountable causation problem because the prosecution need only show that the personality disorder was a but-for cause of the conduct to defeat the defense entirely, regardless of the qualifying disease's role. Under this standard, any causal contribution from the personality disorder precludes a valid insanity plea.

**Answer:** (d)

**Explanation:** Under MPC § 4.01(2), an abnormality manifested "only by repeated criminal or otherwise anti-social conduct" does not qualify as a mental disease. The word "only" does the critical work: a personality disorder standing alone cannot qualify, but it does not disqualify a defendant with a co-occurring mental illness. The exclusion applies to the threshold disease requirement, not causation. If the qualifying disease (schizophrenia) caused the incapacity, the defense survives even if the personality disorder contributed. (a) is incorrect because the MPC excludes abnormalities manifested *only* by antisocial conduct; it is not an absolute bar for co-occurring conditions. (b) is incorrect because the MPC contains no "automatic merger" doctrine; the disorders remain legally distinct for the causation inquiry. (c) is incorrect because a co-occurring psychotic disorder does not automatically satisfy the causation element; the defense must still prove the qualifying disease caused the incapacity. (e) is incorrect because the statute does not require the qualifying disease to be the sole cause, nor does any contribution from a personality disorder defeat the defense entirely if the qualifying disease caused the requisite incapacity.

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [insanity, personality-disorder-exclusion, co-occurring-causation, qualifying-mental-disease], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis, trap: Students treat the personality disorder exclusion as a taint rule

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, personality-disorder-exclusion refinement; co-occurring-causation refinement; *State v. Meiser*.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student might argue that the MPC’s specific exclusion of psychopathic or antisocial personality traits in Section 4.01(2) acts as a strict character taint rule. Because the statute explicitly removes these abnormalities from the definition of a mental disease, any conduct driven by them cannot be excused. Therefore, the mere presence of an antisocial personality disorder acts as an absolute bar to the defense, forcing the defense to concede the point regardless of other diagnoses.
(b) Argument-for: A student could argue that psychiatry is imprecise and the law refuses to artificially bifurcate a single human mind. Under this reasoning, the defense should invoke a merger doctrine, arguing that the MPC does not allow parsing a defendant's intertwined diagnoses. Consequently, the co-occurring disorders automatically merge into one legal category, preventing the prosecution from isolating the personality disorder as the sole driver of the behavior.
(c) Argument-for: A student might assert that severe mental illnesses like schizophrenia inherently override personality disorders in legal significance. Because a psychotic disorder fundamentally fractures reality, its presence renders any non-qualifying personality disorder legally moot. Under this view, the co-occurring psychotic disorder automatically satisfies the causation element for all purposes, making the prosecution's attempt to isolate the personality disorder's causal role completely irrelevant.
(d) Argument-for: This is the correct application of the Model Penal Code. Under MPC § 4.01(2), the exclusion only applies to an abnormality manifested "only" by repeated antisocial conduct. Antisocial personality disorder alone does not qualify, but it does not disqualify a defendant who also suffers from schizophrenia. The exclusion governs the threshold definition of a mental disease, meaning if the qualifying disease (schizophrenia) caused the requisite incapacity, the defense remains valid even if there are concurrent causes.
(e) Argument-for: A student could argue that criminal law causation rules strictly require the legally recognized condition to be the exclusive cause of the harm. If the non-qualifying antisocial personality disorder was a but-for cause of the violent behavior, the qualifying mental disease fails the strict causation test. Under this standard, any causal contribution from the personality disorder breaks the chain, forcing the defense to concede an insurmountable causation problem.

Head-to-head: Option (d) relies on the exact statutory text of MPC § 4.01(2), properly distinguishing between the threshold definition of a mental disease and the subsequent causation inquiry. Options (a), (b), (c), and (e) all invent or distort legal doctrines, clearly flagged by their absolute language. Option (a) invents an absolute bar for personality disorders, directly contradicting the word "only" in the MPC. Option (b) invents an MPC "automatic merger" doctrine. Option (c) falsely claims that a psychotic disorder "automatically satisfies the causation element for all purposes," negating the actual insanity test's causation requirement. Option (e) incorrectly imports a strict sole-cause requirement, falsely claiming that any "but-for" contribution from a non-qualifying disorder entirely defeats the defense.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "the MPC excludes any defendant who has been diagnosed with a personality disorder from raising the defense, regardless of whether they also have a qualifying mental illness." — wrong because MPC § 4.01(2) only excludes abnormalities manifested "only" by antisocial conduct; it does not categorically exclude defendants with co-occurring qualifying diseases.
- (b): "the MPC provides that co-occurring disorders automatically merge into one legal category" — wrong because there is no such "automatic merger" provision in the Model Penal Code; the disorders remain analytically distinct.
- (c): "any co-occurring psychotic disorder automatically satisfies the causation element for all purposes" — wrong because the defendant must still factually prove that the qualifying mental disease caused the specific lack of substantial capacity during the offense.
- (e): "the prosecution need only show that the personality disorder was a but-for cause of the conduct to defeat the defense entirely" — wrong because the MPC does not require the qualifying disease to be the sole cause of the conduct; concurrent causation by a personality disorder does not automatically preclude the defense.

Recommended fix: none
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q9.** Your supervisor asks a question about Markov's co-occurring diagnoses: Assume that the prosecution argues Markov's antisocial personality disorder -- not his schizophrenia -- drove his violent behavior. Under the MPC insanity framework that this jurisdiction follows (Facts 2 and 6), how should the defense respond?

(a) The defense should concede that the co-occurring antisocial personality disorder defeats the insanity defense because the MPC excludes any defendant who has been diagnosed with a personality disorder from raising the defense, regardless of whether they also have a qualifying mental illness. Under this view, the mere presence of a personality disorder acts as an absolute bar to any insanity claim.
(b) The defense should argue that both diagnoses should be treated as a single qualifying mental illness because the MPC provides that co-occurring disorders automatically merge into one legal category when they appear together in the same patient. This supposed merger doctrine prevents the prosecution from attempting to parse the relative causal contributions of the distinct psychiatric conditions.
(c) The defense should argue that the personality disorder exclusion does not apply because Markov's antisocial personality disorder is accompanied by schizophrenia, and any co-occurring psychotic disorder automatically satisfies the causation element for all purposes, making the prosecution's argument about which specific disorder actually "drove" the violent behavior completely legally irrelevant.
(d) The defense should argue that antisocial personality disorder standing alone cannot qualify as a "mental disease or defect" under MPC Section 4.01(2), but the exclusion applies only to the threshold disease requirement. Therefore, if the qualifying disease (schizophrenia) caused the incapacity, the defense legally survives even if the personality disorder also contributed to Markov's behavior. <!-- correct -->
(e) The defense should concede that the co-occurring personality disorder creates an insurmountable causation problem because the prosecution need only show that the personality disorder was a but-for cause of the conduct to defeat the defense entirely, regardless of the qualifying disease's role. Under this standard, any causal contribution from the personality disorder precludes a valid insanity plea.

**Answer:** (d)

**Explanation:** Under MPC § 4.01(2), an abnormality manifested "only by repeated criminal or otherwise anti-social conduct" does not qualify as a mental disease. The word "only" does the critical work: a personality disorder standing alone cannot qualify, but it does not disqualify a defendant with a co-occurring mental illness. The exclusion applies to the threshold disease requirement, not causation. If the qualifying disease (schizophrenia) caused the incapacity, the defense survives even if the personality disorder contributed. (a) is incorrect because the MPC excludes abnormalities manifested *only* by antisocial conduct; it is not an absolute bar for co-occurring conditions. (b) is incorrect because the MPC contains no "automatic merger" doctrine; the disorders remain legally distinct for the causation inquiry. (c) is incorrect because a co-occurring psychotic disorder does not automatically satisfy the causation element; the defense must still prove the qualifying disease caused the incapacity. (e) is incorrect because the statute does not require the qualifying disease to be the sole cause, nor does any contribution from a personality disorder defeat the defense entirely if the qualifying disease caused the requisite incapacity.

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [insanity, personality-disorder-exclusion, co-occurring-causation, qualifying-mental-disease], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis, trap: Students treat the personality disorder exclusion as a taint rule

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, personality-disorder-exclusion refinement; co-occurring-causation refinement; *State v. Meiser*.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student might argue that the MPC’s specific exclusion of psychopathic or antisocial personality traits in Section 4.01(2) acts as a strict character taint rule. Because the statute explicitly removes these abnormalities from the definition of a mental disease, any conduct driven by them cannot be excused. Therefore, the mere presence of an antisocial personality disorder acts as an absolute bar to the defense, forcing the defense to concede the point regardless of other diagnoses.
(b) Argument-for: A student could argue that psychiatry is imprecise and the law refuses to artificially bifurcate a single human mind. Under this reasoning, the defense should invoke a merger doctrine, arguing that the MPC does not allow parsing a defendant's intertwined diagnoses. Consequently, the co-occurring disorders automatically merge into one legal category, preventing the prosecution from isolating the personality disorder as the sole driver of the behavior.
(c) Argument-for: A student might assert that severe mental illnesses like schizophrenia inherently override personality disorders in legal significance. Because a psychotic disorder fundamentally fractures reality, its presence renders any non-qualifying personality disorder legally moot. Under this view, the co-occurring psychotic disorder automatically satisfies the causation element for all purposes, making the prosecution's attempt to isolate the personality disorder's causal role completely irrelevant.
(d) Argument-for: This is the correct application of the Model Penal Code. Under MPC § 4.01(2), the exclusion only applies to an abnormality manifested "only" by repeated antisocial conduct. Antisocial personality disorder alone does not qualify, but it does not disqualify a defendant who also suffers from schizophrenia. The exclusion governs the threshold definition of a mental disease, meaning if the qualifying disease (schizophrenia) caused the requisite incapacity, the defense remains valid even if there are concurrent causes.
(e) Argument-for: A student could argue that criminal law causation rules strictly require the legally recognized condition to be the exclusive cause of the harm. If the non-qualifying antisocial personality disorder was a but-for cause of the violent behavior, the qualifying mental disease fails the strict causation test. Under this standard, any causal contribution from the personality disorder breaks the chain, forcing the defense to concede an insurmountable causation problem.

Head-to-head: Option (d) relies on the exact statutory text of MPC § 4.01(2), properly distinguishing between the threshold definition of a mental disease and the subsequent causation inquiry. Options (a), (b), (c), and (e) all invent or distort legal doctrines, clearly flagged by their absolute language. Option (a) invents an absolute bar for personality disorders, directly contradicting the word "only" in the MPC. Option (b) invents an MPC "automatic merger" doctrine. Option (c) falsely claims that a psychotic disorder "automatically satisfies the causation element for all purposes," negating the actual insanity test's causation requirement. Option (e) incorrectly imports a strict sole-cause requirement, falsely claiming that any "but-for" contribution from a non-qualifying disorder entirely defeats the defense.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "the MPC excludes any defendant who has been diagnosed with a personality disorder from raising the defense, regardless of whether they also have a qualifying mental illness." — wrong because MPC § 4.01(2) only excludes abnormalities manifested "only" by antisocial conduct; it does not categorically exclude defendants with co-occurring qualifying diseases.
- (b): "the MPC provides that co-occurring disorders automatically merge into one legal category" — wrong because there is no such "automatic merger" provision in the Model Penal Code; the disorders remain analytically distinct.
- (c): "any co-occurring psychotic disorder automatically satisfies the causation element for all purposes" — wrong because the defendant must still factually prove that the qualifying mental disease caused the specific lack of substantial capacity during the offense.
- (e): "the prosecution need only show that the personality disorder was a but-for cause of the conduct to defeat the defense entirely" — wrong because the MPC does not require the qualifying disease to be the sole cause of the conduct; concurrent causation by a personality disorder does not automatically preclude the defense.

Recommended fix: none

## Q10

### Grounding

**Q10.** Returning to the full fact pattern, your supervisor asks about Holmes's potential duress defense to a murder charge for the death of the city worker (Fact 5). Under the common law (Fact 6), which of the following is the most accurate analysis?

(a) Holmes has a valid duress defense because the threat was of death to his mother (a close relation), the threat was credible based on Nietzsche's prior conduct, and the common law's "murder bar" is a sentencing-phase consideration that operates only after conviction to limit a defendant's exposure, not a substantive bar at the guilt phase.

(b) Holmes does not have a valid duress defense because under the traditional common law rule, duress is categorically unavailable as a defense to any murder, including felony murder, regardless of how severe or credible the threat. This categorical prohibition operates as an absolute substantive bar to the defense at the guilt phase of the trial.

(c) Holmes has a valid duress defense because the MPC makes duress available for all crimes including murder, and the modern trend favors extending the defense to homicide cases in all jurisdictions. Under this view, the credibility of the threat to his mother outweighs the common law homicide bar, allowing the jury to fully acquit him at trial.

(d) Holmes does not have a valid duress defense because the threat was directed at his mother rather than at Holmes himself, and the common law requires that the threat target the defendant personally before duress can be raised. Even if the threat was highly credible, the defense fails categorically because it lacks this strictly required element.

(e) Holmes has a valid duress defense for the underlying assault but the death triggers the felony murder rule, and duress always breaks the chain of felony murder liability because the predicate felony was coerced in every jurisdiction that recognizes the defense. This exception serves to protect coerced actors from severe homicide consequences.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** Under the traditional common law rule, duress is categorically unavailable as a defense to any murder, including felony murder. This is an absolute bar regardless of the severity or credibility of the threat. Option (a) falsely states that the common law's murder bar is a sentencing-phase consideration; it is actually a categorical substantive rule operating at the guilt phase. Option (c) relies on the MPC rule despite Fact 6 specifying common law and falsely claims a universal modern trend extending the defense to homicide. Option (d) contains a false legal rule: the common law does not require the threat to target the defendant personally and extends duress to threats against close relations. Option (e) falsely claims that duress always breaks the chain of felony murder liability; this exception exists only in a small minority of states and is not the universal common law rule.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, duress-murder-bar, duress-threat-threshold], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application, trap: Students focus on the credible threat and overlook the categorical CL homicide bar

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, duress-murder-bar refinement; felony-murder-duress-exception refinement.

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — duress murder bar, Ch 21, Refinement `duress-murder-bar`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q10.** Returning to the full fact pattern, your supervisor asks about Holmes's potential duress defense to a murder charge for the death of the city worker (Fact 5). Under the common law (Fact 6), which of the following is the most accurate analysis?

(a) Holmes has a valid duress defense because the threat was of death to his mother (a close relation), the threat was credible based on Nietzsche's prior conduct, and the common law's "murder bar" is a sentencing-phase consideration that operates only after conviction to limit a defendant's exposure, not a substantive bar at the guilt phase.

(b) Holmes does not have a valid duress defense because under the traditional common law rule, duress is categorically unavailable as a defense to any murder, including felony murder, regardless of how severe or credible the threat. This categorical prohibition operates as an absolute substantive bar to the defense at the guilt phase of the trial.

(c) Holmes has a valid duress defense because the MPC makes duress available for all crimes including murder, and the modern trend favors extending the defense to homicide cases in all jurisdictions. Under this view, the credibility of the threat to his mother outweighs the common law homicide bar, allowing the jury to fully acquit him at trial.

(d) Holmes does not have a valid duress defense because the threat was directed at his mother rather than at Holmes himself, and the common law requires that the threat target the defendant personally before duress can be raised. Even if the threat was highly credible, the defense fails categorically because it lacks this strictly required element.

(e) Holmes has a valid duress defense for the underlying assault but the death triggers the felony murder rule, and duress always breaks the chain of felony murder liability because the predicate felony was coerced in every jurisdiction that recognizes the defense. This exception serves to protect coerced actors from severe homicide consequences.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** Under the traditional common law rule, duress is categorically unavailable as a defense to any murder, including felony murder. This is an absolute bar regardless of the severity or credibility of the threat. Option (a) falsely states that the common law's murder bar is a sentencing-phase consideration; it is actually a categorical substantive rule operating at the guilt phase. Option (c) relies on the MPC rule despite Fact 6 specifying common law and falsely claims a universal modern trend extending the defense to homicide. Option (d) contains a false legal rule: the common law does not require the threat to target the defendant personally and extends duress to threats against close relations. Option (e) falsely claims that duress always breaks the chain of felony murder liability; this exception exists only in a small minority of states and is not the universal common law rule.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, duress-murder-bar, duress-threat-threshold], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application, trap: Students focus on the credible threat and overlook the categorical CL homicide bar

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, duress-murder-bar refinement; felony-murder-duress-exception refinement.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student might argue that the strict common law rule forbidding duress as a murder defense has been conceptually reimagined in some contexts as an imperfect defense or a sentencing mitigator. To avoid a profoundly unfair result at the guilt phase, courts sometimes treat mitigating factors as reducing culpability rather than barring liability completely. By stating the "murder bar" operates only to limit exposure after conviction, this option offers a plausible compromise. It reconciles the rigid common law tradition with the reality of a highly credible threat to a close relative.
(b) Argument-for: This accurately reflects the core traditional common law doctrine. Under the common law, duress is simply not a defense to intentional murder, nor is it a defense to felony murder. The fundamental rationale is that the law cannot endorse balancing an innocent life against another. Therefore, the defense is categorically unavailable as a substantive bar at the guilt phase. This applies regardless of how severe, imminent, or credible the threat is.
(c) Argument-for: A student could argue that the Model Penal Code represents the definitive modern understanding of duress. Under this view, the MPC's progressive approach has effectively eclipsed the traditional common law in contemporary practice. By pointing to a "modern trend" that allows duress for all crimes, this option appeals to students who believe the common law's rigid murder bar has been universally abandoned. If it were true that all jurisdictions allow this, the jury could fully acquit Holmes.
(d) Argument-for: A student might recall that strict historical common law rules required the threat of harm to be strictly imminent and direct. They could reasonably deduce that the threat must be to the defendant himself rather than a third party to satisfy this immediacy. By arguing that the common law "requires that the threat target the defendant personally," this option provides a technically strict rule-based reason to deny the defense. This sounds like an authentic common law technicality that categorically defeats the defense.
(e) Argument-for: A student could reason that felony murder strictly requires an underlying predicate felony to anchor liability. If that underlying felony is completely excused by duress, the felony murder charge must logically fail because its foundation is gone. By stating that duress "always breaks the chain of felony murder liability," this option provides a compelling logical syllogism. The claim that this happens in "every jurisdiction" sounds like a definitive hornbook rule that cleanly resolves the tension between the murder bar and a coerced predicate act.

Head-to-head: Option (b) is the clear, unambiguous correct answer, correctly applying the traditional common law rule that completely bars duress as a defense to any murder. The distractors are remarkably well-constructed because they all anchor into plausible misunderstandings of doctrine, yet they all feature explicitly false, locked legal statements. Option (a) incorrectly claims the murder bar is just a sentencing-phase consideration. Option (c) applies the MPC despite the prompt specifying common law and falsely claims a universal modern trend in "all jurisdictions." Option (d) falsely claims the common law strictly requires the threat to target the defendant personally. Option (e) uses absolute phrasing to falsely claim duress "always" breaks the felony murder chain in "every jurisdiction," which fundamentally conflicts with the traditional common law rule.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "the common law's 'murder bar' is a sentencing-phase consideration that operates only after conviction... not a substantive bar at the guilt phase" — wrong because the common law murder bar is an absolute substantive defense bar at the guilt phase.
- (c): "the modern trend favors extending the defense to homicide cases in all jurisdictions" — wrong because many jurisdictions, specifically those following the common law, continue to maintain a strict bar against duress for murder.
- (d): "the common law requires that the threat target the defendant personally" — wrong because the common law broadly allows duress to be claimed for threats of death or serious bodily injury to immediate family members or close relations.
- (e): "duress always breaks the chain of felony murder liability because the predicate felony was coerced in every jurisdiction" — wrong because the traditional common law rule (and the majority rule) is that duress is categorically unavailable for murder, including felony murder, meaning it does not break the chain in every jurisdiction.

Recommended fix: none
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q10.** Returning to the full fact pattern, your supervisor asks about Holmes's potential duress defense to a murder charge for the death of the city worker (Fact 5). Under the common law (Fact 6), which of the following is the most accurate analysis?

(a) Holmes has a valid duress defense because the threat was of death to his mother (a close relation), the threat was credible based on Nietzsche's prior conduct, and the common law's "murder bar" is a sentencing-phase consideration that operates only after conviction to limit a defendant's exposure, not a substantive bar at the guilt phase.

(e) Holmes has a valid duress defense for the underlying assault but the death triggers the felony murder rule, and duress always breaks the chain of felony murder liability because the predicate felony was coerced in every jurisdiction that recognizes the defense. This exception serves to protect coerced actors from severe homicide consequences.

## Q11

### Grounding

**Q11.** Kant is charged with intentional criminal mischief and knowing unauthorized possession of a controlled substance for breaking into the Green Street Pharmacy. Kant argues his actions were necessary to prevent a psychotic break. The prosecution counters that Kant created the emergency by letting his prescription expire. How does Kant's fault affect his necessity defense under common law and the Model Penal Code?

(a) The defense is categorically unavailable under *United States v. Oakland Cannabis Buyers' Cooperative* because the scheduling of prescription drugs constitutes a strict legislative preemption of any medical-necessity claim, foreclosing judicial weighing of the harms.
(b) The defense is viable under both standards because averting an expected psychotic break clearly outweighs the minor property damage caused by the break-in, and the immediate unavailability of an ER bed fully satisfies the strict no-legal-alternative element for both.
(c) The defense is viable under the Model Penal Code because Kant's fault in creating the crisis does not bar a defense to intentional or knowing crimes, but it fails under common law, which categorically bars the defense if the defendant contributed to the emergency.
(d) The defense fails under the Model Penal Code because § 3.02 categorically strips the justification defense from any defendant who is reckless or negligent in bringing about the crisis, regardless of whether the defendant is ultimately charged with an intentional crime.
(e) The defense is viable under the Model Penal Code but fails under common law because the traditional common-law standard strictly requires the averted harm to be a threat of death or serious bodily injury, whereas a psychotic break is merely a severe medical condition.

**Answer:** (c)

**Explanation:** Option (c) correctly identifies the MPC/common-law split regarding fault. Under the traditional common law, a defendant must have "clean hands"; contributing to the creation of the emergency categorically bars the necessity defense. Under Model Penal Code § 3.02(2), a defendant's negligence or recklessness in creating the crisis only precludes the defense for crimes where that same level of mens rea suffices for culpability; the defense remains viable for the intentional and knowing crimes charged here. (a) misapplies *Oakland Cannabis*, which requires the legislature to have explicitly weighed the competing medical harms, not merely scheduled a drug. (b) ignores the common-law fault bar, which defeats the defense despite proportionate harms and a lack of alternatives. (d) misstates MPC § 3.02(2), which calibrates against mens rea rather than acting as a categorical bar. (e) fabricates a common-law rule; necessity compares the magnitude of harms and does not categorically require the averted harm to be a threat of death or serious bodily injury.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [necessity, proportionality, legislative-preemption, fault-calibration], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Part I (CL five-element test clean-hands rule, Model Penal Code § 3.02 fault-calibration rule, *Oakland Cannabis* legislative-preemption limitation).

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — Necessity fault calibration (MPC vs. Common Law), Ch 21, Refinement `mpc-fault-calibration`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Ambiguity Audit

**Q11.** Kant is charged with intentional criminal mischief and knowing unauthorized possession of a controlled substance for breaking into the Green Street Pharmacy. Kant argues his actions were necessary to prevent a psychotic break. The prosecution counters that Kant created the emergency by letting his prescription expire. How does Kant's fault affect his necessity defense under common law and the Model Penal Code?

(a) The defense is categorically unavailable under *United States v. Oakland Cannabis Buyers' Cooperative* because the scheduling of prescription drugs constitutes a strict legislative preemption of any medical-necessity claim, foreclosing judicial weighing of the harms.
(b) The defense is viable under both standards because averting an expected psychotic break clearly outweighs the minor property damage caused by the break-in, and the immediate unavailability of an ER bed fully satisfies the strict no-legal-alternative element for both.
(c) The defense is viable under the Model Penal Code because Kant's fault in creating the crisis does not bar a defense to intentional or knowing crimes, but it fails under common law, which categorically bars the defense if the defendant contributed to the emergency.
(d) The defense fails under the Model Penal Code because § 3.02 categorically strips the justification defense from any defendant who is reckless or negligent in bringing about the crisis, regardless of whether the defendant is ultimately charged with an intentional crime.
(e) The defense is viable under the Model Penal Code but fails under common law because the traditional common-law standard strictly requires the averted harm to be a threat of death or serious bodily injury, whereas a psychotic break is merely a severe medical condition.

**Answer:** (c)

**Explanation:** Option (c) correctly identifies the MPC/common-law split regarding fault. Under the traditional common law, a defendant must have "clean hands"; contributing to the creation of the emergency categorically bars the necessity defense. Under Model Penal Code § 3.02(2), a defendant's negligence or recklessness in creating the crisis only precludes the defense for crimes where that same level of mens rea suffices for culpability; the defense remains viable for the intentional and knowing crimes charged here. (a) misapplies *Oakland Cannabis*, which requires the legislature to have explicitly weighed the competing medical harms, not merely scheduled a drug. (b) ignores the common-law fault bar, which defeats the defense despite proportionate harms and a lack of alternatives. (d) misstates MPC § 3.02(2), which calibrates against mens rea rather than acting as a categorical bar. (e) fabricates a common-law rule; necessity compares the magnitude of harms and does not categorically require the averted harm to be a threat of death or serious bodily injury.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [necessity, proportionality, legislative-preemption, fault-calibration], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Part I (CL five-element test clean-hands rule, Model Penal Code § 3.02 fault-calibration rule, *Oakland Cannabis* legislative-preemption limitation).

### Edge Case Audit

**Q11.** Kant is charged with intentional criminal mischief and knowing unauthorized possession of a controlled substance for breaking into the Green Street Pharmacy. Kant argues his actions were necessary to prevent a psychotic break. The prosecution counters that Kant created the emergency by letting his prescription expire. How does Kant's fault affect his necessity defense under common law and the Model Penal Code?

(a) The defense is categorically unavailable under *United States v. Oakland Cannabis Buyers' Cooperative* because the scheduling of prescription drugs constitutes a strict legislative preemption of any medical-necessity claim, foreclosing judicial weighing of the harms.
(b) The defense is viable under both standards because averting an expected psychotic break clearly outweighs the minor property damage caused by the break-in, and the immediate unavailability of an ER bed fully satisfies the strict no-legal-alternative element for both.
(c) The defense is viable under the Model Penal Code because Kant's fault in creating the crisis does not bar a defense to intentional or knowing crimes, but it fails under common law, which categorically bars the defense if the defendant contributed to the emergency.
(d) The defense fails under the Model Penal Code because § 3.02 categorically strips the justification defense from any defendant who is reckless or negligent in bringing about the crisis, regardless of whether the defendant is ultimately charged with an intentional crime.
(e) The defense is viable under the Model Penal Code but fails under common law because the traditional common-law standard strictly requires the averted harm to be a threat of death or serious bodily injury, whereas a psychotic break is merely a severe medical condition.

**Answer:** (c)

**Explanation:** Option (c) correctly identifies the MPC/common-law split regarding fault. Under the traditional common law, a defendant must have "clean hands"; contributing to the creation of the emergency categorically bars the necessity defense. Under Model Penal Code § 3.02(2), a defendant's negligence or recklessness in creating the crisis only precludes the defense for crimes where that same level of mens rea suffices for culpability; the defense remains viable for the intentional and knowing crimes charged here. (a) misapplies *Oakland Cannabis*, which requires the legislature to have explicitly weighed the competing medical harms, not merely scheduled a drug. (b) ignores the common-law fault bar, which defeats the defense despite proportionate harms and a lack of alternatives. (d) misstates MPC § 3.02(2), which calibrates against mens rea rather than acting as a categorical bar. (e) fabricates a common-law rule; necessity compares the magnitude of harms and does not categorically require the averted harm to be a threat of death or serious bodily injury.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [necessity, proportionality, legislative-preemption, fault-calibration], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Part I (CL five-element test clean-hands rule, Model Penal Code § 3.02 fault-calibration rule, *Oakland Cannabis* legislative-preemption limitation).

<!-- edge-case-audit: CLEAN

3. Cross-Question Spoilers: pass
-->

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q11.** Kant is charged with intentional criminal mischief and knowing unauthorized possession of a controlled substance for breaking into the Green Street Pharmacy. Kant argues his actions were necessary to prevent a psychotic break. The prosecution counters that Kant created the emergency by letting his prescription expire. How does Kant's fault affect his necessity defense under common law and the Model Penal Code?

(a) The defense is categorically unavailable under *United States v. Oakland Cannabis Buyers' Cooperative* because the scheduling of prescription drugs constitutes a strict legislative preemption of any medical-necessity claim, foreclosing judicial weighing of the harms.
(b) The defense is viable under both standards because averting an expected psychotic break clearly outweighs the minor property damage caused by the break-in, and the immediate unavailability of an ER bed fully satisfies the strict no-legal-alternative element for both.
(c) The defense is viable under the Model Penal Code because Kant's fault in creating the crisis does not bar a defense to intentional or knowing crimes, but it fails under common law, which categorically bars the defense if the defendant contributed to the emergency.
(d) The defense fails under the Model Penal Code because § 3.02 categorically strips the justification defense from any defendant who is reckless or negligent in bringing about the crisis, regardless of whether the defendant is ultimately charged with an intentional crime.
(e) The defense is viable under the Model Penal Code but fails under common law because the traditional common-law standard strictly requires the averted harm to be a threat of death or serious bodily injury, whereas a psychotic break is merely a severe medical condition.

**Answer:** (c)

**Explanation:** Option (c) correctly identifies the MPC/common-law split regarding fault. Under the traditional common law, a defendant must have "clean hands"; contributing to the creation of the emergency categorically bars the necessity defense. Under Model Penal Code § 3.02(2), a defendant's negligence or recklessness in creating the crisis only precludes the defense for crimes where that same level of mens rea suffices for culpability; the defense remains viable for the intentional and knowing crimes charged here. (a) misapplies *Oakland Cannabis*, which requires the legislature to have explicitly weighed the competing medical harms, not merely scheduled a drug. (b) ignores the common-law fault bar, which defeats the defense despite proportionate harms and a lack of alternatives. (d) misstates MPC § 3.02(2), which calibrates against mens rea rather than acting as a categorical bar. (e) fabricates a common-law rule; necessity compares the magnitude of harms and does not categorically require the averted harm to be a threat of death or serious bodily injury.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [necessity, proportionality, legislative-preemption, fault-calibration], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Part I (CL five-element test clean-hands rule, Model Penal Code § 3.02 fault-calibration rule, *Oakland Cannabis* legislative-preemption limitation).

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: Under *United States v. Oakland Cannabis Buyers' Cooperative*, the Supreme Court held that a medical necessity defense cannot succeed when the legislature has already weighed the harms by classifying a substance under the Controlled Substances Act. Since Kant is charged with unauthorized possession of a controlled substance, a student could argue that the legislative scheduling categorically preempts the judicial weighing of harms required for a necessity defense. In this view, any claim of medical necessity is foreclosed by the statutory scheme.
(b) Argument-for: The core of the necessity defense is the choice of evils. A student could argue that averting a severe medical crisis like a psychotic break clearly outweighs the minor property damage of breaking into a pharmacy and the victimless crime of possessing a drug. Since Kant had no legal alternative (assuming no ER beds were available), the defense's primary elements are satisfied. Thus, the defense should be viable under both the common law and MPC, treating his failure to renew his prescription as mere background context rather than an absolute bar.
(c) Argument-for: This option correctly identifies the MPC's nuanced approach to fault in creating the emergency. Under traditional common law, a defendant must have "clean hands"; contributing to the crisis entirely bars the necessity defense. However, MPC § 3.02(2) calibrates fault: if the defendant is negligent or reckless in bringing about the situation, the defense is only barred for crimes where negligence or recklessness suffices. Because Kant is charged with intentional criminal mischief and knowing possession, his lesser fault in creating the emergency does not bar his defense.
(d) Argument-for: A student could interpret the MPC's fault provision as a strict, categorical rule against rewarding defendants who create their own emergencies. If a defendant's negligence or recklessness brings about the crisis, they shouldn't be allowed to invoke necessity to escape punishment for subsequent intentional acts. Therefore, one could argue that § 3.02 categorically strips the justification defense from any defendant who is at fault, regardless of the mens rea of the ultimate charge.
(e) Argument-for: Common law justification defenses are often narrower than the MPC. A student could conflate the elements of necessity with duress, or assume that traditional courts strictly limited necessity to physical emergencies. Under this reasoning, common law necessity strictly requires an imminent threat of death or serious bodily injury, meaning a psychiatric emergency like a psychotic break would only qualify under the broader "choice of evils" standard of the MPC.

Head-to-head: Option (c) accurately captures the legal distinction between the common law's absolute fault bar and the MPC's mens rea-calibrated fault rule. Option (a) overextends *Oakland Cannabis*, which only applies to Schedule I drugs (where Congress explicitly found "no currently accepted medical use"), not standard prescription drugs. Option (b) falsely claims viability under both standards, ignoring the common-law absolute bar for creating the emergency. Option (d) misstates the MPC rule by making it a categorical bar, directly contradicting the statutory text of § 3.02(2). Option (e) falsely imports the "death or serious bodily injury" requirement from duress into common-law necessity, which generally allows any lesser harm to be chosen over a greater harm. All distractors contain clear, falsifiable legal errors locked with absolute phrasing.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "scheduling of prescription drugs constitutes a strict legislative preemption of any medical-necessity claim" — wrong because *Oakland Cannabis* applies only to Schedule I drugs (which have "no currently accepted medical use" by definition), not to the mere scheduling of any prescription drug.
- (b): "viable under both standards" — wrong because the traditional common law categorically bars the necessity defense if the defendant contributed to creating the emergency.
- (d): "categorically strips the justification defense... regardless of whether the defendant is ultimately charged with an intentional crime" — wrong because MPC § 3.02(2) explicitly preserves the necessity defense for intentional/knowing crimes if the defendant was merely reckless/negligent in creating the crisis.
- (e): "traditional common-law standard strictly requires the averted harm to be a threat of death or serious bodily injury" — wrong because common-law necessity compares the relative magnitude of harms and is not strictly limited to threats of death or serious bodily injury.

Recommended fix: none
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q11.** Kant is charged with intentional criminal mischief and knowing unauthorized possession of a controlled substance for breaking into the Green Street Pharmacy. Kant argues his actions were necessary to prevent a psychotic break. The prosecution counters that Kant created the emergency by letting his prescription expire. How does Kant's fault affect his necessity defense under common law and the Model Penal Code?

(a) The defense is categorically unavailable under *United States v. Oakland Cannabis Buyers' Cooperative* because the scheduling of prescription drugs constitutes a strict legislative preemption of any medical-necessity claim, foreclosing judicial weighing of the harms.

(c) The defense is viable under the Model Penal Code because Kant's fault in creating the crisis does not bar a defense to intentional or knowing crimes, but it fails under common law, which categorically bars the defense if the defendant contributed to the emergency.
(d) The defense fails under the Model Penal Code because § 3.02 categorically strips the justification defense from any defendant who is reckless or negligent in bringing about the crisis, regardless of whether the defendant is ultimately charged with an intentional crime.
(e) The defense is viable under the Model Penal Code but fails under common law because the traditional common-law standard strictly requires the averted harm to be a threat of death or serious bodily injury, whereas a psychotic break is merely a severe medical condition.

**Answer:** (c)

**Explanation:** Option (c) correctly identifies the MPC/common-law split regarding fault. Under the traditional common law, a defendant must have "clean hands"; contributing to the creation of the emergency categorically bars the necessity defense. ## Q12

### Grounding

**Q12.** Assume it is established that Kant drove the crew's van to the First Federal depot on March 15. Recall that on March 10, Bentham threatened that if Kant refused to drive, Jarvis (who had a documented history of sending associates to the hospital for surgical repair) would put Kant in the hospital for a week, and that Kant's mother would "go next." Evaluate Kant's duress defense under both common-law and Model Penal Code standards, focusing on the threat threshold and the target scope.

(a) The threat threshold and target scope elements are satisfied under both standards: Bentham's threatened beating via Jarvis qualifies as serious bodily injury under the common law and as unlawful force under the Model Penal Code, and the threat against Kant's mother reaches a "close relation" at common law and "any person" under the Model Penal Code.
(b) The target scope element fails under the common law because threats against third parties — including a defendant's mother — cannot satisfy the common-law target element, which strictly limits cognizable threats to force directed at the defendant personally, meaning any coercive pressure exerted by threatening a relative is deemed legally insufficient.
(c) The threat threshold fails under the Model Penal Code because Bentham's threats were not strictly imminent — Bentham promised future retaliation days later rather than immediate on-the-spot force — and the Model Penal Code ultimately retains the common-law imminence requirement to ensure the threshold element rests on an unavoidable present danger.
(d) The threat threshold is satisfied under the Model Penal Code's broader standard but fails under the common law, because the common-law threshold strictly requires an immediate gun-to-the-head scenario of deadly force, meaning that mere verbal threats of a severe beating by an enforcer cannot ever qualify as serious bodily injury for this defense.
(e) The threat threshold fails under both standards because Bentham's threats clearly do not satisfy the required "reasonable firmness" test: an ordinary person with five days' notice would have reported these threats to the police before March 15, thereby immediately rendering the underlying threat insufficient as a strict matter of law prior to trial.

**Answer:** (a)

**Explanation:** (a) correctly applies the rules. The common law requires threats of "death or serious bodily injury"; Bentham's threatened beating via Jarvis qualifies, and the secondary threat against Kant's mother reaches a "close relation." The MPC sets a broader threshold ("unlawful force" against "any person"), which is plainly satisfied. (b) falsely narrows the common-law target scope, which expressly extends to "close relations," including parents. (c) falsely imports strict imminence into the MPC; § 2.09 drops the strict imminence requirement, though time delay remains relevant to reasonableness. (d) fabricates a strict "gun-to-the-head" rule; threats of grievous bodily harm satisfy the common-law threshold. (e) is incorrect because under the common law, failure to report goes to the separate "no reasonable opportunity to escape" element, while under the MPC, the "reasonable firmness" test is generally a factual question for the jury rather than a matter of law.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, threat-threshold, target-scope, imminence], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Part II (CL/Model Penal Code duress comparison table; *Contento-Pachon* on imminence; *Dingwall* on corroborating patterns of violence).

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — duress, Ch 21, Refinement `duress-threat-threshold` and `duress-target-scope`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Ambiguity Audit

**Q12.** Assume it is established that Kant drove the crew's van to the First Federal depot on March 15. Recall that on March 10, Bentham threatened that if Kant refused to drive, Jarvis (who had a documented history of sending associates to the hospital for surgical repair) would put Kant in the hospital for a week, and that Kant's mother would "go next." (b) falsely narrows the common-law target scope, which expressly extends to "close relations," including parents. (c) falsely imports strict imminence into the MPC; § 2.09 drops the strict imminence requirement, though time delay remains relevant to reasonableness. (d) fabricates a strict "gun-to-the-head" rule; threats of grievous bodily harm satisfy the common-law threshold. (e) is incorrect because under the common law, failure to report goes to the separate "no reasonable opportunity to escape" element, while under the MPC, the "reasonable firmness" test is generally a factual question for the jury rather than a matter of law.

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q12.** Assume it is established that Kant drove the crew's van to the First Federal depot on March 15. Recall that on March 10, Bentham threatened that if Kant refused to drive, Jarvis (who had a documented history of sending associates to the hospital for surgical repair) would put Kant in the hospital for a week, and that Kant's mother would "go next." (a) The threat threshold and target scope elements are satisfied under both standards: Bentham's threatened beating via Jarvis qualifies as serious bodily injury under the common law and as unlawful force under the Model Penal Code, and the threat against Kant's mother reaches a "close relation" at common law and "any person" under the Model Penal Code.

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Part II (CL/Model Penal Code duress comparison table; *Contento-Pachon* on imminence; *Dingwall* on corroborating patterns of violence).

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: Option (a) accurately applies the black-letter rules for duress under both the common law and the Model Penal Code. Under the common law, the threat must be of death or serious bodily injury, and a week-long hospital stay inflicted by a known violent enforcer clearly qualifies as serious bodily harm. Furthermore, the common law traditionally recognizes threats against a "close relation," such as a parent. Finally, the MPC's broader standard requires only a threat of "unlawful force" against "any person," which is easily satisfied by Bentham's threats against both Kant and his mother.

(b) Argument-for: Option (b) could be argued as correct by appealing to the strictest historical common-law formulations of duress, which demanded that the threat of death or great bodily harm be directed at the defendant himself. A student could argue that extending the scope to third parties is a modern MPC innovation rather than a universal common-law rule. By this logic, the coercive pressure from a threat against Kant's mother fails the target scope element. Therefore, any reliance on the threat to the mother is legally insufficient under an orthodox strict reading of the common law.

(c) Argument-for: Option (c) draws upon the fact that the gap between March 10 and March 15 strongly implicates imminence and the opportunity to escape. A student could argue that while the MPC alters the formulation of the defense, it effectively retains the imminence requirement through the "person of reasonable firmness" standard, as no reasonable person would yield to a non-imminent threat without seeking help. Because Bentham promised retaliation days later rather than immediate on-the-spot force, the threat lacked immediacy. Thus, the threshold fails under the MPC because the danger was not an unavoidable present danger.

(d) Argument-for: Option (d) asserts a rigid interpretation of the common-law threshold for duress, which was historically reserved for extreme, immediate threats of death. A student might argue that a mere verbal threat of a beating—even a severe one by an enforcer—does not rise to the level of death or the kind of catastrophic bodily harm traditionally required, such as a literal gun to the head. Because the common law strongly disfavors the duress defense, this strict threshold ensures that only the most undeniably overpowering threats qualify. Therefore, Bentham's threat falls short of the common-law standard but satisfies the MPC's "unlawful force" standard.

(e) Argument-for: Option (e) focuses on the five-day gap between the threat and the crime, arguing that this delay defeats the defense under both standards as a strict matter of law. Under the common law, a five-day gap provides ample reasonable opportunity to escape or report to the police, which operates as an absolute bar. Under the MPC, a person of "reasonable firmness" would undoubtedly report a five-day advance threat to authorities rather than commit a bank robbery. Because the facts establish a five-day notice period, a judge would preclude the defense before it ever reaches the jury, making both thresholds fail strictly as a matter of law.

Head-to-head: Option (a) accurately aligns the facts with the standard elements for duress under both the common law and the MPC. Option (b) invents a falsifiable claim that the common law "strictly limits" cognizable threats to the defendant personally, explicitly contradicting the accepted common-law inclusion of "close relations." Option (c) fails because it asserts the MPC "ultimately retains the common-law imminence requirement," which is a direct misstatement of MPC § 2.09. Option (d) uses absolutist phrasing ("strictly requires an immediate gun-to-the-head scenario") to falsely claim that severe beatings cannot satisfy the common-law serious bodily injury threshold. Option (e) falsely collapses the "no reasonable opportunity to escape" element into the threat threshold and incorrectly asserts that a five-day delay automatically precludes the MPC defense "as a strict matter of law prior to trial," stripping the jury of its fact-finding role on "reasonable firmness." All distractors are firmly locked with explicit false legal claims.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (b): "strictly limits cognizable threats to force directed at the defendant personally" — wrong because the common law expressly recognizes threats directed at "close relations" such as parents, spouses, and children.
- (c): "the Model Penal Code ultimately retains the common-law imminence requirement" — wrong because the MPC deliberately abandons the strict imminence requirement in favor of a general "person of reasonable firmness" standard.
- (d): "strictly requires an immediate gun-to-the-head scenario of deadly force, meaning that mere verbal threats of a severe beating... cannot ever qualify" — wrong because the common law explicitly accepts threats of "serious bodily injury" or "grievous bodily harm," not just threats of death or gun-to-the-head scenarios.
- (e): "immediately rendering the underlying threat insufficient as a strict matter of law prior to trial" — wrong because time delay relates to the distinct "no reasonable opportunity to escape" element at common law (not the threat threshold), and presents a factual jury question regarding "reasonable firmness" under the MPC rather than an automatic legal bar prior to trial.

Recommended fix: none
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q12.** Assume it is established that Kant drove the crew's van to the First Federal depot on March 15. Recall that on March 10, Bentham threatened that if Kant refused to drive, Jarvis (who had a documented history of sending associates to the hospital for surgical repair) would put Kant in the hospital for a week, and that Kant's mother would "go next." Evaluate Kant's duress defense under both common-law and Model Penal Code standards, focusing on the threat threshold and the target scope.

(a) The threat threshold and target scope elements are satisfied under both standards: Bentham's threatened beating via Jarvis qualifies as serious bodily injury under the common law and as unlawful force under the Model Penal Code, and the threat against Kant's mother reaches a "close relation" at common law and "any person" under the Model Penal Code.
(b) The target scope element fails under the common law because threats against third parties — including a defendant's mother — cannot satisfy the common-law target element, which strictly limits cognizable threats to force directed at the defendant personally, meaning any coercive pressure exerted by threatening a relative is deemed legally insufficient.
(c) The threat threshold fails under the Model Penal Code because Bentham's threats were not strictly imminent — Bentham promised future retaliation days later rather than immediate on-the-spot force — and the Model Penal Code ultimately retains the common-law imminence requirement to ensure the threshold element rests on an unavoidable present danger.
(d) The threat threshold is satisfied under the Model Penal Code's broader standard but fails under the common law, because the common-law threshold strictly requires an immediate gun-to-the-head scenario of deadly force, meaning that mere verbal threats of a severe beating by an enforcer cannot ever qualify as serious bodily injury for this defense.
(e) The threat threshold fails under both standards because Bentham's threats clearly do not satisfy the required "reasonable firmness" test: an ordinary person with five days' notice would have reported these threats to the police before March 15, thereby immediately rendering the underlying threat insufficient as a strict matter of law prior to trial.

**Answer:** (a)

**Explanation:** (a) correctly applies the rules. The common law requires threats of "death or serious bodily injury"; Bentham's threatened beating via Jarvis qualifies, and the secondary threat against Kant's mother reaches a "close relation." The MPC sets a broader threshold ("unlawful force" against "any person"), which is plainly satisfied. (b) falsely narrows the common-law target scope, which expressly extends to "close relations," including parents. (c) falsely imports strict imminence into the MPC; § 2.09 drops the strict imminence requirement, though time delay remains relevant to reasonableness. (d) fabricates a strict "gun-to-the-head" rule; threats of grievous bodily harm satisfy the common-law threshold. (e) is incorrect because under the common law, failure to report goes to the separate "no reasonable opportunity to escape" element, while under the MPC, the "reasonable firmness" test is generally a factual question for the jury rather than a matter of law.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, threat-threshold, target-scope, imminence], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Part II (CL/Model Penal Code duress comparison table; *Contento-Pachon* on imminence; *Dingwall* on corroborating patterns of violence).

## Q13

### Grounding

**Q13.** Assume Kant is charged with murder for Bayes's death based on his participation in the armored-car robbery. Relying solely on the provided text of Model Penal Code § 2.09, which of the following is the most accurate analysis of Kant's potential duress defense to the murder charge?

(a) Kant's duress defense is categorically barred because, while the Model Penal Code generally recognizes duress, it implicitly incorporates the traditional absolute prohibition against asserting this excuse for any homicide offense, thereby requiring Kant to face liability regardless of the threat's severity.
(b) Kant's duress defense is fully available to be presented to the jury because Section 2.09 contains no categorical homicide exception, allowing the defense for any crime if a person of reasonable firmness in Kant's exact situation would have been unable to resist the severe physical threat to his mother. <!-- correct -->
(c) Kant's duress defense is strictly barred because Section 2.09 expressly limits the defense to situations where the unlawful force is threatened against the actor personally, meaning the statute categorically precludes any excuse based on severe threats directed toward the defendant's family members.
(d) Kant's duress defense is fully available but only if he can prove the harm avoided (the severe injury to his mother) quantitatively outweighed the harm caused (the death of the guard), because the Model Penal Code explicitly merges duress into the choice of evils balancing test for all homicide charges.
(e) Kant's duress defense is automatically successful because the Model Penal Code explicitly provides that any credible threat of serious bodily injury by an armed co-felon creates a binding, irrebuttable presumption that a reasonable person would comply, shielding non-shooter participants from murder.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** (b) is correct. Unlike the traditional common law, Model Penal Code § 2.09 contains no categorical exception for murder. The defense is available for any crime, including homicide, if a person of reasonable firmness in the actor's situation would have been unable to resist the threat. Option (a) is incorrect because the MPC explicitly abandoned the absolute homicide bar. Option (c) is incorrect because § 2.09 expressly extends the defense to threats against "the person of another," not just the defendant personally. Option (d) is incorrect because the MPC treats duress (§ 2.09) as a distinct excuse evaluated under a "reasonable firmness" standard, rather than merging it into the § 3.02 choice of evils justification test requiring a strict quantitative balancing of harms. Option (e) is incorrect because the MPC does not create an irrebuttable presumption for armed co-felon threats; the jury must always evaluate the coercion under the objective "person of reasonable firmness" standard.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, duress-murder-bar, mpc-reasonable-firmness], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Problem 7 and Part II review (Model Penal Code § 2.09 abandons the CL murder bar; "person of another" scope; reasonable firmness standard).

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — MPC Duress rules, Ch 21, Refinement `duress-murder-bar`, `duress-target-scope`, and `reasonable-firmness-objective`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

(e) Kant's duress defense is automatically successful because the Model Penal Code explicitly provides that any credible threat of serious bodily injury by an armed co-felon creates a binding, irrebuttable presumption that a reasonable person would comply, shielding non-shooter participants from murder.

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q13.** Assume Kant is charged with murder for Bayes's death based on his participation in the armored-car robbery. Relying solely on the provided text of Model Penal Code § 2.09, which of the following is the most accurate analysis of Kant's potential duress defense to the murder charge?

(a) Kant's duress defense is categorically barred because, while the Model Penal Code generally recognizes duress, it implicitly incorporates the traditional absolute prohibition against asserting this excuse for any homicide offense, thereby requiring Kant to face liability regardless of the threat's severity.
(b) Kant's duress defense is fully available to be presented to the jury because Section 2.09 contains no categorical homicide exception, allowing the defense for any crime if a person of reasonable firmness in Kant's exact situation would have been unable to resist the severe physical threat to his mother. <!-- correct -->
(c) Kant's duress defense is strictly barred because Section 2.09 expressly limits the defense to situations where the unlawful force is threatened against the actor personally, meaning the statute categorically precludes any excuse based on severe threats directed toward the defendant's family members.
(d) Kant's duress defense is fully available but only if he can prove the harm avoided (the severe injury to his mother) quantitatively outweighed the harm caused (the death of the guard), because the Model Penal Code explicitly merges duress into the choice of evils balancing test for all homicide charges.
(e) Kant's duress defense is automatically successful because the Model Penal Code explicitly provides that any credible threat of serious bodily injury by an armed co-felon creates a binding, irrebuttable presumption that a reasonable person would comply, shielding non-shooter participants from murder.

Option (e) is incorrect because the MPC does not create an irrebuttable presumption for armed co-felon threats; the jury must always evaluate the coercion under the objective "person of reasonable firmness" standard.

**Tags:** chapters: [21], topics: [duress, duress-murder-bar, mpc-reasonable-firmness], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 21, Problem 7 and Part II review (Model Penal Code § 2.09 abandons the CL murder bar; "person of another" scope; reasonable firmness standard).

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student might select this option by relying on the traditional common-law rule that duress is never a defense to murder. They could argue that because murder is the most serious offense, the Model Penal Code must have implicitly retained this deeply entrenched historical bar. By characterizing the MPC's silence on a murder exception as an implicit incorporation of the common law, this option appeals to a student who recalls the traditional Blackstonian prohibition. Thus, they could plausibly conclude that Kant is categorically barred from asserting duress for Bayes's death.
(b) Argument-for: This option correctly tracks the explicit language and structure of Model Penal Code § 2.09. A student would note that the provision allows a defense for any offense if a person of "reasonable firmness" would be unable to resist the threat. The student would also recognize that the MPC deliberately abandoned the common-law exception for homicide, making the defense fully available to be presented to the jury. Finally, the inclusion of threats against "another" squarely covers the threat to Kant's mother, making this the most legally accurate analysis.
(c) Argument-for: A student could be drawn to this option if they assume criminal excuses are construed as narrowly as possible. They might argue that the Model Penal Code requires the threat to be directed at the defendant to ensure the immediacy and severity of the coercion. Under this rationale, threats against family members, while tragic, might be viewed as legally insufficient to justify participating in a felony that leads to murder. This creates a superficially plausible statutory limit that tests a student's precise memory of the phrase "person of another."
(d) Argument-for: This option appeals to students who conflate the justification of necessity (choice of evils) with the excuse of duress. A student might argue that because a life was taken, the only way to excuse it is if the harm avoided strictly outweighed the harm caused. They could conclude that the Model Penal Code must explicitly merge these concepts when homicide is involved, thereby forcing a quantitative balancing test before allowing a duress defense. This presents a strong conceptual distractor by mixing MPC § 3.02 logic into MPC § 2.09.
(e) Argument-for: A student might choose this option if they believe the Model Penal Code offers absolute protection to non-shooters coerced by armed accomplices. They could argue that a severe, credible threat of serious bodily injury from a co-felon satisfies the "reasonable firmness" standard as a matter of law. By framing the statutory standard as an irrebuttable presumption, the student could conclude that Kant is automatically shielded from murder liability. This tempts students who over-apply the defense to situations where the coercion seems overwhelmingly strong.

Head-to-head: 
Option (b) is the clear winner because it accurately states the Model Penal Code § 2.09 standard, acknowledging the "reasonable firmness" test, the inclusion of threats to a third party, and the lack of a homicide bar. The distractors all utilize absolute, falsifiable phrasing regarding the text of the MPC. Option (a) incorrectly claims the MPC "implicitly incorporates" the common-law homicide bar. Option (c) falsely asserts the statute "expressly limits" the defense to personal threats. Option (d) erroneously claims the MPC "explicitly merges" duress into choice of evils for homicide charges. Option (e) fabricates an explicit "binding, irrebuttable presumption." Because every distractor makes a falsifiable claim about what the MPC explicitly or implicitly states, the question strictly separates the correct answer from the wrong ones.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "implicitly incorporates the traditional absolute prohibition against asserting this excuse for any homicide offense" — wrong because MPC § 2.09 does not incorporate this prohibition, but rather deliberately abandons the common-law homicide exception.
- (c): "expressly limits the defense to situations where the unlawful force is threatened against the actor personally" — wrong because the text of § 2.09 explicitly extends to threats against "the person of another."
- (d): "explicitly merges duress into the choice of evils balancing test for all homicide charges" — wrong because the MPC treats duress (§ 2.09) and choice of evils (§ 3.02) as distinct defenses and lacks any explicit merger provision for homicide.
- (e): "explicitly provides that any credible threat... creates a binding, irrebuttable presumption" — wrong because § 2.09 provides an objective "person of reasonable firmness" standard to be evaluated by the jury, rather than an automatic irrebuttable presumption.

Recommended fix: none
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q13.** Assume Kant is charged with murder for Bayes's death based on his participation in the armored-car robbery. Relying solely on the provided text of Model Penal Code § 2.09, which of the following is the most accurate analysis of Kant's potential duress defense to the murder charge?

(a) Kant's duress defense is categorically barred because, while the Model Penal Code generally recognizes duress, it implicitly incorporates the traditional absolute prohibition against asserting this excuse for any homicide offense, thereby requiring Kant to face liability regardless of the threat's severity.
(b) Kant's duress defense is fully available to be presented to the jury because Section 2.09 contains no categorical homicide exception, allowing the defense for any crime if a person of reasonable firmness in Kant's exact situation would have been unable to resist the severe physical threat to his mother. <!-- correct -->
(c) Kant's duress defense is strictly barred because Section 2.09 expressly limits the defense to situations where the unlawful force is threatened against the actor personally, meaning the statute categorically precludes any excuse based on severe threats directed toward the defendant's family members.
(d) Kant's duress defense is fully available but only if he can prove the harm avoided (the severe injury to his mother) quantitatively outweighed the harm caused (the death of the guard), because the Model Penal Code explicitly merges duress into the choice of evils balancing test for all homicide charges.
