## Q19

### Grounding

**Q19.** Following his altercation with Officer Hand during a psychotic episode, Kant was evaluated by a court-appointed psychiatrist, Dr. Laplace, who concluded that Kant's symptoms rendered him "unable to conform his conduct to the requirements of law and substantially impaired his appreciation of the wrongfulness of his act." Assume Kant's schizophrenia qualifies as a "mental disease or defect." If Kant's defense attorney compares how this evidence will fare under the Model Penal Code § 4.01 insanity test versus the federal Insanity Defense Reform Act (18 U.S.C. § 17), which of the following conclusions is most accurate?

(a) Under the Insanity Defense Reform Act, Kant would easily prevail because the federal statute, enacted in the wake of the John Hinckley acquittal, expressly adopted the Model Penal Code's volitional prong in 1984. Therefore, Dr. Laplace's explicit clinical finding that Kant was completely "unable to conform his conduct to the requirements of law" perfectly tracks that federal statutory language, granting him a complete defense regardless of his cognitive appreciation of the wrongfulness of the act.
(b) Under the Model Penal Code, Kant satisfies both independent prongs—he lacked substantial capacity to appreciate wrongfulness and lacked substantial capacity to conform his conduct to the law—and need satisfy only one. Under the Insanity Defense Reform Act, Kant faces a higher hurdle: he must establish a "severe" mental disease, total inability (not substantial impairment) to appreciate the nature or wrongfulness of the act by clear and convincing evidence, and the volitional prong is unavailable. <!-- correct -->
(c) Under the Model Penal Code, Kant must definitively show he was totally "unable" to appreciate the wrongfulness of his actions or conform his conduct to the law. Consequently, Dr. Laplace's finding of "substantial impairment" is legally insufficient as a matter of law, because mere substantial impairment of cognitive or volitional functions falls far short of the total, absolute incapacity that the Model Penal Code strictly requires defendants to demonstrate to establish an affirmative defense.
(d) Under both the Model Penal Code and the federal Insanity Defense Reform Act, the procedural and substantive burdens rest entirely on the defendant to prove insanity by a preponderance of the evidence. Dr. Laplace's clear, uncontested psychiatric testimony confirming both cognitive and volitional impairments satisfies that uniform evidentiary standard across jurisdictions, meaning Kant will establish an insanity defense without needing to meet a higher "clear and convincing" threshold in federal court.
(e) The Model Penal Code's volitional prong was recently held unconstitutional by the Supreme Court in *Kahler v. Kansas*, which ruled that the Due Process Clause fundamentally precludes any affirmative insanity defense that extends beyond the mere negation of mens rea. As a direct consequence of this binding constitutional precedent, the volitional prong—which focuses on the defendant's ability to control their behavior—is therefore no longer legally available to assert in any state or federal court.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** (b) correctly distinguishes the two tests. The MPC requires only that the defendant lack substantial capacity to either appreciate wrongfulness or conform conduct to law; Kant meets both disjunctive prongs based on Dr. Laplace's findings. The IDRA tightened the federal standard, requiring "severe" mental disease, total inability to appreciate wrongfulness, dropping the volitional prong entirely, and shifting the burden to the defendant by clear and convincing evidence. (a) falsely states the IDRA adopted the volitional prong; it explicitly eliminated it. (c) falsely elevates the MPC requirement to total incapacity, whereas the MPC only requires "substantial capacity." (d) falsely equates the burden-of-proof standards; the IDRA requires clear and convincing evidence, not preponderance. (e) misstates *Kahler v. Kansas*, which permitted states to abolish the affirmative insanity defense but did not constitutionalize that choice or invalidate the MPC prongs.

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [mpc-appreciate-criminality, federal-idra-requirements, burden-allocation-post-hinckley, volitional-prong], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, Section C (Model Penal Code § 4.01 substantial-capacity test); Section D (Insanity Defense Reform Act, 18 U.S.C. § 17: severe disease, cognitive-only, clear and convincing); Section on Kahler v. Kansas.

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — MPC vs IDRA Insanity Tests, Ch 23, Refinements `mpc-appreciate-criminality`, `mpc-conform-conduct`, `federal-idra-requirements`, and `burden-allocation-post-hinckley`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Ambiguity Audit

**Q19.** Following his altercation with Officer Hand during a psychotic episode, Kant was evaluated by a court-appointed psychiatrist, Dr. Laplace, who concluded that Kant's symptoms rendered him "unable to conform his conduct to the requirements of law and substantially impaired his appreciation of the wrongfulness of his act." Assume Kant's schizophrenia qualifies as a "mental disease or defect." If Kant's defense attorney compares how this evidence will fare under the Model Penal Code § 4.01 insanity test versus the federal Insanity Defense Reform Act (18 U.S.C. § 17), which of the following conclusions is most accurate?

(a) Under the Insanity Defense Reform Act, Kant would easily prevail because the federal statute, enacted in the wake of the John Hinckley acquittal, expressly adopted the Model Penal Code's volitional prong in 1984. Therefore, Dr. Laplace's explicit clinical finding that Kant was completely "unable to conform his conduct to the requirements of law" perfectly tracks that federal statutory language, granting him a complete defense regardless of his cognitive appreciation of the wrongfulness of the act.
(b) Under the Model Penal Code, Kant satisfies both independent prongs—he lacked substantial capacity to appreciate wrongfulness and lacked substantial capacity to conform his conduct to the law—and need satisfy only one. Under the Insanity Defense Reform Act, Kant faces a higher hurdle: he must establish a "severe" mental disease, total inability (not substantial impairment) to appreciate the nature or wrongfulness of the act by clear and convincing evidence, and the volitional prong is unavailable. <!-- correct -->
(c) Under the Model Penal Code, Kant must definitively show he was totally "unable" to appreciate the wrongfulness of his actions or conform his conduct to the law. Consequently, Dr. Laplace's finding of "substantial impairment" is legally insufficient as a matter of law, because mere substantial impairment of cognitive or volitional functions falls far short of the total, absolute incapacity that the Model Penal Code strictly requires defendants to demonstrate to establish an affirmative defense.
(d) Under both the Model Penal Code and the federal Insanity Defense Reform Act, the procedural and substantive burdens rest entirely on the defendant to prove insanity by a preponderance of the evidence. Dr. Laplace's clear, uncontested psychiatric testimony confirming both cognitive and volitional impairments satisfies that uniform evidentiary standard across jurisdictions, meaning Kant will establish an insanity defense without needing to meet a higher "clear and convincing" threshold in federal court.
(e) The Model Penal Code's volitional prong was recently held unconstitutional by the Supreme Court in *Kahler v. Kansas*, which ruled that the Due Process Clause fundamentally precludes any affirmative insanity defense that extends beyond the mere negation of mens rea. As a direct consequence of this binding constitutional precedent, the volitional prong—which focuses on the defendant's ability to control their behavior—is therefore no longer legally available to assert in any state or federal court.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** (b) correctly distinguishes the two tests. The MPC requires only that the defendant lack substantial capacity to either appreciate wrongfulness or conform conduct to law; Kant meets both disjunctive prongs based on Dr. Laplace's findings. The IDRA tightened the federal standard, requiring "severe" mental disease, total inability to appreciate wrongfulness, dropping the volitional prong entirely, and shifting the burden to the defendant by clear and convincing evidence. (a) falsely states the IDRA adopted the volitional prong; it explicitly eliminated it. (c) falsely elevates the MPC requirement to total incapacity, whereas the MPC only requires "substantial capacity." (d) falsely equates the burden-of-proof standards; the IDRA requires clear and convincing evidence, not preponderance. (e) misstates *Kahler v. Kansas*, which permitted states to abolish the affirmative insanity defense but did not constitutionalize that choice or invalidate the MPC prongs.

### Edge Case Audit

**Q19.** Following his altercation with Officer Hand during a psychotic episode, Kant was evaluated by a court-appointed psychiatrist, Dr. Laplace, who concluded that Kant's symptoms rendered him "unable to conform his conduct to the requirements of law and substantially impaired his appreciation of the wrongfulness of his act." Assume Kant's schizophrenia qualifies as a "mental disease or defect." If Kant's defense attorney compares how this evidence will fare under the Model Penal Code § 4.01 insanity test versus the federal Insanity Defense Reform Act (18 U.S.C. § 17), which of the following conclusions is most accurate?

(a) Under the Insanity Defense Reform Act, Kant would easily prevail because the federal statute, enacted in the wake of the John Hinckley acquittal, expressly adopted the Model Penal Code's volitional prong in 1984. Therefore, Dr. Laplace's explicit clinical finding that Kant was completely "unable to conform his conduct to the requirements of law" perfectly tracks that federal statutory language, granting him a complete defense regardless of his cognitive appreciation of the wrongfulness of the act.
(b) Under the Model Penal Code, Kant satisfies both independent prongs—he lacked substantial capacity to appreciate wrongfulness and lacked substantial capacity to conform his conduct to the law—and need satisfy only one. Under the Insanity Defense Reform Act, Kant faces a higher hurdle: he must establish a "severe" mental disease, total inability (not substantial impairment) to appreciate the nature or wrongfulness of the act by clear and convincing evidence, and the volitional prong is unavailable. <!-- correct -->
(c) Under the Model Penal Code, Kant must definitively show he was totally "unable" to appreciate the wrongfulness of his actions or conform his conduct to the law. Consequently, Dr. Laplace's finding of "substantial impairment" is legally insufficient as a matter of law, because mere substantial impairment of cognitive or volitional functions falls far short of the total, absolute incapacity that the Model Penal Code strictly requires defendants to demonstrate to establish an affirmative defense.
(d) Under both the Model Penal Code and the federal Insanity Defense Reform Act, the procedural and substantive burdens rest entirely on the defendant to prove insanity by a preponderance of the evidence. Dr. Laplace's clear, uncontested psychiatric testimony confirming both cognitive and volitional impairments satisfies that uniform evidentiary standard across jurisdictions, meaning Kant will establish an insanity defense without needing to meet a higher "clear and convincing" threshold in federal court.
(e) The Model Penal Code's volitional prong was recently held unconstitutional by the Supreme Court in *Kahler v. Kansas*, which ruled that the Due Process Clause fundamentally precludes any affirmative insanity defense that extends beyond the mere negation of mens rea. As a direct consequence of this binding constitutional precedent, the volitional prong—which focuses on the defendant's ability to control their behavior—is therefore no longer legally available to assert in any state or federal court.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** (b) correctly distinguishes the two tests. The MPC requires only that the defendant lack substantial capacity to either appreciate wrongfulness or conform conduct to law; Kant meets both disjunctive prongs based on Dr. Laplace's findings. The IDRA tightened the federal standard, requiring "severe" mental disease, total inability to appreciate wrongfulness, dropping the volitional prong entirely, and shifting the burden to the defendant by clear and convincing evidence. (a) falsely states the IDRA adopted the volitional prong; it explicitly eliminated it. (c) falsely elevates the MPC requirement to total incapacity, whereas the MPC only requires "substantial capacity." (d) falsely equates the burden-of-proof standards; the IDRA requires clear and convincing evidence, not preponderance. (e) misstates *Kahler v. Kansas*, which permitted states to abolish the affirmative insanity defense but did not constitutionalize that choice or invalidate the MPC prongs.

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [mpc-appreciate-criminality, federal-idra-requirements, burden-allocation-post-hinckley, volitional-prong], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, Section C (Model Penal Code § 4.01 substantial-capacity test); Section D (Insanity Defense Reform Act, 18 U.S.C. § 17: severe disease, cognitive-only, clear and convincing); Section on Kahler v. Kansas.

<!-- edge-case-audit: MUST FIX
1. Fact Pattern Booby Traps: pass
2. Cross-Doctrine Clashes: pass
3. Cross-Question Spoilers: Q7 completely spoils Q19 (and vice versa). Both questions test the exact same list of divergence points between the MPC and IDRA standards. Q7(d) explicitly lists that IDRA requires a "severe" disease, eliminates the volitional prong, replaces "substantial capacity" with "unable," and requires clear and convincing evidence. Q19(b) uses almost this exact wording. A student reading Q7 is directly handed the answer to Q19.
Recommended fix: Retarget Q19 to test a different legal issue raised by the fact pattern, such as the deific decree doctrine/moral vs. legal wrongness distinction triggered by Kant's demonic possession delusion in Facts 12 and 13, rather than re-testing the statutory MPC vs. IDRA comparison.

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q19.** Following his altercation with Officer Hand during a psychotic episode, Kant was evaluated by a court-appointed psychiatrist, Dr. Laplace, who concluded that Kant's symptoms rendered him "unable to conform his conduct to the requirements of law and substantially impaired his appreciation of the wrongfulness of his act." Assume Kant's schizophrenia qualifies as a "mental disease or defect." If Kant's defense attorney compares how this evidence will fare under the Model Penal Code § 4.01 insanity test versus the federal Insanity Defense Reform Act (18 U.S.C. § 17), which of the following conclusions is most accurate?

(a) Under the Insanity Defense Reform Act, Kant would easily prevail because the federal statute, enacted in the wake of the John Hinckley acquittal, expressly adopted the Model Penal Code's volitional prong in 1984. Therefore, Dr. Laplace's explicit clinical finding that Kant was completely "unable to conform his conduct to the requirements of law" perfectly tracks that federal statutory language, granting him a complete defense regardless of his cognitive appreciation of the wrongfulness of the act.
(b) Under the Model Penal Code, Kant satisfies both independent prongs—he lacked substantial capacity to appreciate wrongfulness and lacked substantial capacity to conform his conduct to the law—and need satisfy only one. Consequently, Dr. Laplace's finding of "substantial impairment" is legally insufficient as a matter of law, because mere substantial impairment of cognitive or volitional functions falls far short of the total, absolute incapacity that the Model Penal Code strictly requires defendants to demonstrate to establish an affirmative defense.
(d) Under both the Model Penal Code and the federal Insanity Defense Reform Act, the procedural and substantive burdens rest entirely on the defendant to prove insanity by a preponderance of the evidence. (e) The Model Penal Code's volitional prong was recently held unconstitutional by the Supreme Court in *Kahler v. Kansas*, which ruled that the Due Process Clause fundamentally precludes any affirmative insanity defense that extends beyond the mere negation of mens rea. As a direct consequence of this binding constitutional precedent, the volitional prong—which focuses on the defendant's ability to control their behavior—is therefore no longer legally available to assert in any state or federal court.

**Answer:** (b)

**Explanation:** (b) correctly distinguishes the two tests. (d) falsely equates the burden-of-proof standards; the IDRA requires clear and convincing evidence, not preponderance. (e) misstates *Kahler v. **Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [mpc-appreciate-criminality, federal-idra-requirements, burden-allocation-post-hinckley, volitional-prong], difficulty: hard, cognitive: analysis

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, Section C (Model Penal Code § 4.01 substantial-capacity test); Section D (Insanity Defense Reform Act, 18 U.S.C. § 17: severe disease, cognitive-only, clear and convincing); Section on Kahler v. Kansas.

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: A student could defend this by pointing out that the federal Insanity Defense Reform Act was indeed enacted in the wake of the John Hinckley acquittal in 1984. They might erroneously conclude that to "reform" the defense, Congress codified the modern Model Penal Code standard, which includes the volitional prong. Therefore, Dr. Laplace's finding that Kant was "unable to conform his conduct" would perfectly satisfy this assumed federal standard. Under this misapprehension, the student would logically deduce that Kant has a complete defense under the IDRA regardless of his cognitive state.
(b) Argument-for: This is the correct option and relies on a precise understanding of the differences between the MPC and the IDRA. Under the MPC (§ 4.01), a defendant only needs to lack "substantial capacity" in either the cognitive or volitional prong, which Kant meets based on the psychiatrist's findings. Conversely, the IDRA requires a "severe" mental disease, restricts the defense entirely to the cognitive prong (requiring the defendant to be "unable" to appreciate wrongfulness, rather than lacking substantial capacity), and shifts the burden to the defendant by clear and convincing evidence. This answer accurately maps Kant's clinical findings to the distinct hurdles of both standards.
(c) Argument-for: A student might argue for (c) by confusing the Model Penal Code with the strict, traditional M'Naghten rule. Since strict cognitive tests often require a defendant to totally lack knowledge of the nature or wrongfulness of their act, the student could assume the MPC maintains this absolute threshold. They would therefore conclude that Dr. Laplace's finding of mere "substantial impairment" is insufficient as a matter of law. This argument rests on the false premise that the MPC strictly requires "total, absolute incapacity."
(d) Argument-for: A student could justify (d) by generalizing the standard burden of proof for affirmative defenses—often a preponderance of the evidence—to the federal system. They might recognize that the IDRA shifted the burden to the defendant post-Hinckley but mistakenly assume the burden was set at a preponderance rather than the higher "clear and convincing" standard. If both jurisdictions merely required a preponderance of the evidence, Dr. Laplace's uncontested testimony would indeed easily satisfy the burden for Kant uniformly across the board.
(e) Argument-for: A student might select (e) by misinterpreting the Supreme Court's ruling in *Kahler v. Kansas*. Because *Kahler* held that the Due Process Clause does not compel states to retain an affirmative insanity defense (allowing states to adopt a strict *mens rea* approach), a student might conflate this holding with a constitutional prohibition. They could argue that the Court explicitly struck down broader tests like the MPC's volitional prong, rendering them categorically unavailable as a matter of constitutional law in any court.

Head-to-head: Option (b) is the clear correct answer because it accurately identifies the substantive and procedural distinctions between the MPC and the IDRA. Distractor (a) fails by explicitly claiming the IDRA adopted the volitional prong, which it actually eliminated. Distractor (c) is falsifiable because it claims the MPC requires total incapacity, directly contradicting the explicit "substantial capacity" text of MPC § 4.01. Distractor (d) fails because it asserts the IDRA requires a preponderance of the evidence, whereas 18 U.S.C. § 17 explicitly commands a clear and convincing evidence standard. Distractor (e) is falsifiable because it claims *Kahler* held the volitional prong unconstitutional, whereas *Kahler* merely held that states are not constitutionally required to adopt it. All distractors contain explicit, identifiable false legal claims properly locked by absolute wording.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "expressly adopted the Model Penal Code's volitional prong in 1984" — wrong because the IDRA explicitly eliminated the volitional prong from federal court, restricting the defense solely to the cognitive prong.
- (c): "the total, absolute incapacity that the Model Penal Code strictly requires" — wrong because the MPC expressly requires only that the defendant lack "substantial capacity," deliberately rejecting a total inability standard.
- (d): "without needing to meet a higher 'clear and convincing' threshold in federal court" — wrong because the IDRA (18 U.S.C. § 17) explicitly imposes a "clear and convincing evidence" standard for the insanity defense.
- (e): "The Model Penal Code's volitional prong was recently held unconstitutional by the Supreme Court in Kahler v. Kansas" — wrong because *Kahler* held only that the Constitution does not mandate an affirmative insanity defense, not that existing affirmative defenses (like the MPC test) are unconstitutional.

Recommended fix: none
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q19.** Following his altercation with Officer Hand during a psychotic episode, Kant was evaluated by a court-appointed psychiatrist, Dr. Laplace, who concluded that Kant's symptoms rendered him "unable to conform his conduct to the requirements of law and substantially impaired his appreciation of the wrongfulness of his act." Assume Kant's schizophrenia qualifies as a "mental disease or defect." If Kant's defense attorney compares how this evidence will fare under the Model Penal Code § 4.01 insanity test versus the federal Insanity Defense Reform Act (18 U.S.C. § 17), which of the following conclusions is most accurate?

(a) Under the Insanity Defense Reform Act, Kant would easily prevail because the federal statute, enacted in the wake of the John Hinckley acquittal, expressly adopted the Model Penal Code's volitional prong in 1984. Therefore, Dr. Laplace's explicit clinical finding that Kant was completely "unable to conform his conduct to the requirements of law" perfectly tracks that federal statutory language, granting him a complete defense regardless of his cognitive appreciation of the wrongfulness of the act.
(b) Under the Model Penal Code, Kant satisfies both independent prongs—he lacked substantial capacity to appreciate wrongfulness and lacked substantial capacity to conform his conduct to the law—and need satisfy only one. Under the Insanity Defense Reform Act, Kant faces a higher hurdle: he must establish a "severe" mental disease, total inability (not substantial impairment) to appreciate the nature or wrongfulness of the act by clear and convincing evidence, and the volitional prong is unavailable. <!-- correct -->
(c) Under the Model Penal Code, Kant must definitively show he was totally "unable" to appreciate the wrongfulness of his actions or conform his conduct to the law. Consequently, Dr. Laplace's finding of "substantial impairment" is legally insufficient as a matter of law, because mere substantial impairment of cognitive or volitional functions falls far short of the total, absolute incapacity that the Model Penal Code strictly requires defendants to demonstrate to establish an affirmative defense.
(d) Under both the Model Penal Code and the federal Insanity Defense Reform Act, the procedural and substantive burdens rest entirely on the defendant to prove insanity by a preponderance of the evidence. Dr. Laplace's clear, uncontested psychiatric testimony confirming both cognitive and volitional impairments satisfies that uniform evidentiary standard across jurisdictions, meaning Kant will establish an insanity defense without needing to meet a higher "clear and convincing" threshold in federal court.
(e) The Model Penal Code's volitional prong was recently held unconstitutional by the Supreme Court in *Kahler v. Kansas*, which ruled that the Due Process Clause fundamentally precludes any affirmative insanity defense that extends beyond the mere negation of mens rea. As a direct consequence of this binding constitutional precedent, the volitional prong—which focuses on the defendant's ability to control their behavior—is therefore no longer legally available to assert in any state or federal court.

(c) falsely elevates the MPC requirement to total incapacity, whereas the MPC only requires "substantial capacity." (d) falsely equates the burden-of-proof standards; the IDRA requires clear and convincing evidence, not preponderance. (e) misstates *Kahler v. Kansas*, which permitted states to abolish the affirmative insanity defense but did not constitutionalize that choice or invalidate the MPC prongs.

## Q20

### Grounding

**Q20.** Assume that the jury at Kant's trial for the attempted murder of Officer Hand is considering whether to return a verdict of not guilty by reason of insanity. Evaluate the civil-commitment consequences of a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict and the operation of a Mutina-style jury instruction designed to address the misconception that a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict means immediate release.

(a) A verdict of not guilty by reason of insanity results in the defendant's immediate release, because acquittal by reason of insanity is constitutionally equivalent to any other acquittal under the Double Jeopardy Clause; civil commitment proceedings, if any, must be initiated separately, with the defendant released pending their outcome, which means a Mutina-style instruction is legally inaccurate and impermissibly prejudices the jury by suggesting a loss of liberty that the trial court cannot guarantee.

(b) Commitment duration following a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict is capped at the maximum sentence the defendant could have received upon conviction; upon expiration, the acquittee must be released regardless of clinical status, meaning that a Mutina-style instruction must specifically inform the jury of this strict statutory maximum so that jurors do not operate under the false assumption that an acquittee will face a lifetime of indefinite confinement in a state psychiatric hospital.

(c) In every jurisdiction that recognizes the not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict, a psychiatric security review board composed of mental-health professionals has exclusive authority to order release, and courts are categorically barred from overriding the board's decisions; accordingly, a Mutina-style instruction is generally utilized to assure jurors that release decisions will be handled exclusively by clinical experts rather than by judges who lack specialized medical or psychiatric training.

(d) A verdict of not guilty by reason of insanity almost always results in involuntary commitment to a secure psychiatric facility, often for a period equal to or longer than the prison sentence the defendant would have received on conviction; a Mutina-style instruction informs the jury that a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict does not mean immediate release and that commitment is the ordinary disposition, in order to dispel a pervasive misconception that acquittal equals freedom.

(e) The Mutina-style instruction was held unconstitutional in *Kahler v. Kansas* because it improperly informs the jury of sentencing consequences; its use in any jurisdiction therefore constitutes reversible error, as the Supreme Court concluded that providing jurors with detailed information about post-verdict civil-commitment timelines inappropriately invites them to base their ultimate determination of criminal responsibility on public safety concerns rather than the defendant's mental state at the time of the offense.

**Answer:** (d)

**Explanation:** (d) correctly describes both the empirical reality and the purpose of the Mutina-style instruction. Acquittees found not guilty by reason of insanity are ordinarily committed to secure psychiatric facilities, with duration tied to clinical status rather than the hypothetical prison sentence; in practice, commitment is frequently as long as or longer than the underlying sentence would have been. The Mutina-style instruction addresses the well-documented misconception that a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict means release, which jurors consistently overestimate (actual not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity rate is ~0.26%). (a) falsely treats a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict as an ordinary acquittal for liberty purposes; nearly all jurisdictions provide for automatic commitment proceedings, not release. (b) fabricates a sentence-based cap; commitment duration is tied to clinical status and can be effectively indefinite in many jurisdictions. (c) falsely universalizes the psychiatric-security-review-board model; only a small number of states (notably Oregon and Connecticut) use psychiatric security review boards with direct release authority, and in most jurisdictions, judges retain final release authority. (e) fabricates a *Kahler* holding on jury instructions; *Kahler* addressed only whether states may abolish the moral-wrongness insanity defense, not the use of instructions about commitment consequences.

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [NGRI-civil-commitment, Mutina-instruction, release-procedures, public-misconception], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, Section "What Happens After Acquittal" (automatic commitment; duration tied to clinical status; judicial v. PSRB release models); Section "The Empirical Reality of the Insanity Defense" (0.26% NGRI rate; Mutina-style instruction corrective purpose).

<!-- grounding: GROUNDED — Mutina instruction and NGRI civil commitment, Ch 23, Refinement `mutina-instruction` and `ngri-civil-commitment`. Rule paraphrased accurately. -->

### Ambiguity Audit

**Q20.** Assume that the jury at Kant's trial for the attempted murder of Officer Hand is considering whether to return a verdict of not guilty by reason of insanity. Evaluate the civil-commitment consequences of a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict and the operation of a Mutina-style jury instruction designed to address the misconception that a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict means immediate release.

(a) A verdict of not guilty by reason of insanity results in the defendant's immediate release, because acquittal by reason of insanity is constitutionally equivalent to any other acquittal under the Double Jeopardy Clause; civil commitment proceedings, if any, must be initiated separately, with the defendant released pending their outcome, which means a Mutina-style instruction is legally inaccurate and impermissibly prejudices the jury by suggesting a loss of liberty that the trial court cannot guarantee.

(b) Commitment duration following a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict is capped at the maximum sentence the defendant could have received upon conviction; upon expiration, the acquittee must be released regardless of clinical status, meaning that a Mutina-style instruction must specifically inform the jury of this strict statutory maximum so that jurors do not operate under the false assumption that an acquittee will face a lifetime of indefinite confinement in a state psychiatric hospital.

(c) In every jurisdiction that recognizes the not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict, a psychiatric security review board composed of mental-health professionals has exclusive authority to order release, and courts are categorically barred from overriding the board's decisions; accordingly, a Mutina-style instruction is generally utilized to assure jurors that release decisions will be handled exclusively by clinical experts rather than by judges who lack specialized medical or psychiatric training.

(d) A verdict of not guilty by reason of insanity almost always results in involuntary commitment to a secure psychiatric facility, often for a period equal to or longer than the prison sentence the defendant would have received on conviction; a Mutina-style instruction informs the jury that a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict does not mean immediate release and that commitment is the ordinary disposition, in order to dispel a pervasive misconception that acquittal equals freedom.

(e) The Mutina-style instruction was held unconstitutional in *Kahler v. Kansas* because it improperly informs the jury of sentencing consequences; its use in any jurisdiction therefore constitutes reversible error, as the Supreme Court concluded that providing jurors with detailed information about post-verdict civil-commitment timelines inappropriately invites them to base their ultimate determination of criminal responsibility on public safety concerns rather than the defendant's mental state at the time of the offense.

**Answer:** (d)

**Explanation:** (d) correctly describes both the empirical reality and the purpose of the Mutina-style instruction. Acquittees found not guilty by reason of insanity are ordinarily committed to secure psychiatric facilities, with duration tied to clinical status rather than the hypothetical prison sentence; in practice, commitment is frequently as long as or longer than the underlying sentence would have been. The Mutina-style instruction addresses the well-documented misconception that a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict means release, which jurors consistently overestimate (actual not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity rate is ~0.26%). (a) falsely treats a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict as an ordinary acquittal for liberty purposes; nearly all jurisdictions provide for automatic commitment proceedings, not release. (b) fabricates a sentence-based cap; commitment duration is tied to clinical status and can be effectively indefinite in many jurisdictions. (c) falsely universalizes the psychiatric-security-review-board model; only a small number of states (notably Oregon and Connecticut) use psychiatric security review boards with direct release authority, and in most jurisdictions, judges retain final release authority. (e) fabricates a *Kahler* holding on jury instructions; *Kahler* addressed only whether states may abolish the moral-wrongness insanity defense, not the use of instructions about commitment consequences.

### Edge Case Audit

**Q20.** Assume that the jury at Kant's trial for the attempted murder of Officer Hand is considering whether to return a verdict of not guilty by reason of insanity. Evaluate the civil-commitment consequences of a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict and the operation of a Mutina-style jury instruction designed to address the misconception that a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict means immediate release.

(a) A verdict of not guilty by reason of insanity results in the defendant's immediate release, because acquittal by reason of insanity is constitutionally equivalent to any other acquittal under the Double Jeopardy Clause; civil commitment proceedings, if any, must be initiated separately, with the defendant released pending their outcome, which means a Mutina-style instruction is legally inaccurate and impermissibly prejudices the jury by suggesting a loss of liberty that the trial court cannot guarantee.

(b) Commitment duration following a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict is capped at the maximum sentence the defendant could have received upon conviction; upon expiration, the acquittee must be released regardless of clinical status, meaning that a Mutina-style instruction must specifically inform the jury of this strict statutory maximum so that jurors do not operate under the false assumption that an acquittee will face a lifetime of indefinite confinement in a state psychiatric hospital.

(c) In every jurisdiction that recognizes the not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict, a psychiatric security review board composed of mental-health professionals has exclusive authority to order release, and courts are categorically barred from overriding the board's decisions; accordingly, a Mutina-style instruction is generally utilized to assure jurors that release decisions will be handled exclusively by clinical experts rather than by judges who lack specialized medical or psychiatric training.

(d) A verdict of not guilty by reason of insanity almost always results in involuntary commitment to a secure psychiatric facility, often for a period equal to or longer than the prison sentence the defendant would have received on conviction; a Mutina-style instruction informs the jury that a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict does not mean immediate release and that commitment is the ordinary disposition, in order to dispel a pervasive misconception that acquittal equals freedom.

(e) The Mutina-style instruction was held unconstitutional in *Kahler v. Kansas* because it improperly informs the jury of sentencing consequences; its use in any jurisdiction therefore constitutes reversible error, as the Supreme Court concluded that providing jurors with detailed information about post-verdict civil-commitment timelines inappropriately invites them to base their ultimate determination of criminal responsibility on public safety concerns rather than the defendant's mental state at the time of the offense.

**Answer:** (d)

**Explanation:** (d) correctly describes both the empirical reality and the purpose of the Mutina-style instruction. Acquittees found not guilty by reason of insanity are ordinarily committed to secure psychiatric facilities, with duration tied to clinical status rather than the hypothetical prison sentence; in practice, commitment is frequently as long as or longer than the underlying sentence would have been. The Mutina-style instruction addresses the well-documented misconception that a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict means release, which jurors consistently overestimate (actual not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity rate is ~0.26%). (a) falsely treats a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict as an ordinary acquittal for liberty purposes; nearly all jurisdictions provide for automatic commitment proceedings, not release. (b) fabricates a sentence-based cap; commitment duration is tied to clinical status and can be effectively indefinite in many jurisdictions. (c) falsely universalizes the psychiatric-security-review-board model; only a small number of states (notably Oregon and Connecticut) use psychiatric security review boards with direct release authority, and in most jurisdictions, judges retain final release authority. (e) fabricates a *Kahler* holding on jury instructions; *Kahler* addressed only whether states may abolish the moral-wrongness insanity defense, not the use of instructions about commitment consequences.

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [NGRI-civil-commitment, Mutina-instruction, release-procedures, public-misconception], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, Section "What Happens After Acquittal" (automatic commitment; duration tied to clinical status; judicial v. PSRB release models); Section "The Empirical Reality of the Insanity Defense" (0.26% NGRI rate; Mutina-style instruction corrective purpose).

### Argument Pass (Sonnet)

**Q20.** Assume that the jury at Kant's trial for the attempted murder of Officer Hand is considering whether to return a verdict of not guilty by reason of insanity. Evaluate the civil-commitment consequences of a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict and the operation of a Mutina-style jury instruction designed to address the misconception that a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict means immediate release.

(a) A verdict of not guilty by reason of insanity results in the defendant's immediate release, because acquittal by reason of insanity is constitutionally equivalent to any other acquittal under the Double Jeopardy Clause; civil commitment proceedings, if any, must be initiated separately, with the defendant released pending their outcome, which means a Mutina-style instruction is legally inaccurate and impermissibly prejudices the jury by suggesting a loss of liberty that the trial court cannot guarantee.

(b) Commitment duration following a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict is capped at the maximum sentence the defendant could have received upon conviction; upon expiration, the acquittee must be released regardless of clinical status, meaning that a Mutina-style instruction must specifically inform the jury of this strict statutory maximum so that jurors do not operate under the false assumption that an acquittee will face a lifetime of indefinite confinement in a state psychiatric hospital.

(c) In every jurisdiction that recognizes the not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict, a psychiatric security review board composed of mental-health professionals has exclusive authority to order release, and courts are categorically barred from overriding the board's decisions; accordingly, a Mutina-style instruction is generally utilized to assure jurors that release decisions will be handled exclusively by clinical experts rather than by judges who lack specialized medical or psychiatric training.

(d) A verdict of not guilty by reason of insanity almost always results in involuntary commitment to a secure psychiatric facility, often for a period equal to or longer than the prison sentence the defendant would have received on conviction; a Mutina-style instruction informs the jury that a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict does not mean immediate release and that commitment is the ordinary disposition, in order to dispel a pervasive misconception that acquittal equals freedom.

(e) The Mutina-style instruction was held unconstitutional in *Kahler v. Kansas* because it improperly informs the jury of sentencing consequences; its use in any jurisdiction therefore constitutes reversible error, as the Supreme Court concluded that providing jurors with detailed information about post-verdict civil-commitment timelines inappropriately invites them to base their ultimate determination of criminal responsibility on public safety concerns rather than the defendant's mental state at the time of the offense.

**Answer:** (d)

**Explanation:** (d) correctly describes both the empirical reality and the purpose of the Mutina-style instruction. Acquittees found not guilty by reason of insanity are ordinarily committed to secure psychiatric facilities, with duration tied to clinical status rather than the hypothetical prison sentence; in practice, commitment is frequently as long as or longer than the underlying sentence would have been. The Mutina-style instruction addresses the well-documented misconception that a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict means release, which jurors consistently overestimate (actual not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity rate is ~0.26%). (a) falsely treats a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict as an ordinary acquittal for liberty purposes; nearly all jurisdictions provide for automatic commitment proceedings, not release. (b) fabricates a sentence-based cap; commitment duration is tied to clinical status and can be effectively indefinite in many jurisdictions. (c) falsely universalizes the psychiatric-security-review-board model; only a small number of states (notably Oregon and Connecticut) use psychiatric security review boards with direct release authority, and in most jurisdictions, judges retain final release authority. (e) fabricates a *Kahler* holding on jury instructions; *Kahler* addressed only whether states may abolish the moral-wrongness insanity defense, not the use of instructions about commitment consequences.

**Tags:** chapters: [23], topics: [NGRI-civil-commitment, Mutina-instruction, release-procedures, public-misconception], difficulty: medium, cognitive: application

**Grounding:** Chapter 23, Section "What Happens After Acquittal" (automatic commitment; duration tied to clinical status; judicial v. PSRB release models); Section "The Empirical Reality of the Insanity Defense" (0.26% NGRI rate; Mutina-style instruction corrective purpose).

<!-- argument-pass: CLEAN
(a) Argument-for: Under the principles of due process, an acquittal typically restores a defendant's presumption of liberty. One could argue that an NGRI acquittee must therefore be immediately released until separate, formal civil commitment proceedings are successfully concluded under *O'Connor v. Donaldson* standards. Because the trial court cannot guarantee the outcome of a separate civil proceeding, a *Mutina*-style instruction is arguably prejudicial because it falsely promises a loss of liberty that the court has no constitutional power to impose automatically. Thus, (a) presents a plausible argument about the tension between an acquittal and involuntary confinement.

(b) Argument-for: Due process principles often require that the nature and duration of commitment bear a reasonable relation to its purpose. Some jurisdictions have addressed equal protection concerns by tying NGRI commitments to the maximum sentence the defendant would have served if convicted, aiming to treat acquittees and convicts similarly. If a jurisdiction applied this strict maximum, a *Mutina*-style instruction would need to inform the jury of the cap so they do not wrongly assume the commitment is permanent. Option (b) articulates a recognizable theoretical approach to proportionality in commitment duration.

(c) Argument-for: The creation of a Psychiatric Security Review Board (PSRB) is a well-regarded modern approach to handling NGRI acquittees, pioneered by states like Oregon. The core premise of the PSRB model is to transfer release authority from the judiciary to a panel of experts who are better equipped to evaluate clinical dangerousness. To completely insulate decisions from political pressure, courts would logically be barred from overriding the board. Option (c) could be defended by emphasizing the PSRB's specialized release authority and connecting it to the *Mutina* instruction's goal of reassuring the jury.

(d) Argument-for: Option (d) correctly states the empirical reality and legal consequence of an NGRI verdict, which in the vast majority of jurisdictions results in automatic or near-automatic commitment (*Jones v. United States*). Because the duration of commitment is tied to clinical recovery (dangerousness and mental illness) rather than a penal sentence, NGRI acquittees frequently spend longer confined than they would have in prison. To counteract the pervasive "insanity defense myth" that acquittees go free immediately, jurisdictions utilize the *Mutina*-style instruction to truthfully inform the jury about the commitment consequence, preventing wrongful convictions based on unfounded public-safety fears.

(e) Argument-for: The U.S. Supreme Court's jurisprudence generally dictates that juries are not supposed to be instructed on the sentencing or post-verdict consequences of their decisions (*Shannon v. United States*). One could argue that *Kahler v. Kansas*, which addressed the constitutional limits of the insanity defense, implicitly reinforced the idea that providing jurors with detailed commitment timelines invites them to decide criminal responsibility on impermissible public safety grounds. Thus, option (e) provides a plausible constitutional argument that *Mutina*-style instructions improperly conflate the jury's fact-finding role with dispositional consequences.

Head-to-head: Option (d) is legally and empirically accurate, capturing the holding of *Jones v. United States* (permitting automatic commitment and indefinite duration tied to clinical status) and the specific corrective purpose of the *Mutina* instruction. All distractors contain lock-solid, falsifiable legal or historical errors. Option (a) incorrectly equates an NGRI verdict with a general acquittal, ignoring *Jones*. Option (b) falsely fabricates a universal, strict statutory cap based on maximum sentences. Option (c) uses absolute language ("In every jurisdiction... categorically barred") to describe the PSRB model, which is actually only used in a small minority of states. Option (e) completely fabricates a holding for *Kahler v. Kansas*, which dealt with the abolition of the moral-incapacity prong of the insanity defense, not jury instructions.

Falsifiable claim per distractor:
- (a): "results in the defendant's immediate release, because acquittal by reason of insanity is constitutionally equivalent to any other acquittal" — wrong because *Jones v. United States* constitutionally permits automatic commitment for NGRI acquittees.
- (b): "Commitment duration following a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict is capped at the maximum sentence... must be released regardless of clinical status" — wrong because NGRI commitment duration is fundamentally tied to the acquittee's clinical status (mental illness and dangerousness), not a strict penal maximum.
- (c): "In every jurisdiction... a psychiatric security review board... has exclusive authority to order release" — wrong because only a minority of jurisdictions use PSRBs; most retain judicial release authority.
- (e): "The Mutina-style instruction was held unconstitutional in Kahler v. Kansas" — wrong because *Kahler* did not address jury instructions on civil commitment consequences.

Recommended fix: none
-->

### Argument Pass (Opus)

**Q20.** Assume that the jury at Kant's trial for the attempted murder of Officer Hand is considering whether to return a verdict of not guilty by reason of insanity. Evaluate the civil-commitment consequences of a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict and the operation of a Mutina-style jury instruction designed to address the misconception that a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict means immediate release.

(a) A verdict of not guilty by reason of insanity results in the defendant's immediate release, because acquittal by reason of insanity is constitutionally equivalent to any other acquittal under the Double Jeopardy Clause; civil commitment proceedings, if any, must be initiated separately, with the defendant released pending their outcome, which means a Mutina-style instruction is legally inaccurate and impermissibly prejudices the jury by suggesting a loss of liberty that the trial court cannot guarantee.

(b) Commitment duration following a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict is capped at the maximum sentence the defendant could have received upon conviction; upon expiration, the acquittee must be released regardless of clinical status, meaning that a Mutina-style instruction must specifically inform the jury of this strict statutory maximum so that jurors do not operate under the false assumption that an acquittee will face a lifetime of indefinite confinement in a state psychiatric hospital.

(c) In every jurisdiction that recognizes the not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict, a psychiatric security review board composed of mental-health professionals has exclusive authority to order release, and courts are categorically barred from overriding the board's decisions; accordingly, a Mutina-style instruction is generally utilized to assure jurors that release decisions will be handled exclusively by clinical experts rather than by judges who lack specialized medical or psychiatric training.

(d) A verdict of not guilty by reason of insanity almost always results in involuntary commitment to a secure psychiatric facility, often for a period equal to or longer than the prison sentence the defendant would have received on conviction; a Mutina-style instruction informs the jury that a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict does not mean immediate release and that commitment is the ordinary disposition, in order to dispel a pervasive misconception that acquittal equals freedom.

(e) The Mutina-style instruction was held unconstitutional in *Kahler v. Kansas* because it improperly informs the jury of sentencing consequences; its use in any jurisdiction therefore constitutes reversible error, as the Supreme Court concluded that providing jurors with detailed information about post-verdict civil-commitment timelines inappropriately invites them to base their ultimate determination of criminal responsibility on public safety concerns rather than the defendant's mental state at the time of the offense.

**Answer:** (d)

**Explanation:** (d) correctly describes both the empirical reality and the purpose of the Mutina-style instruction. (a) falsely treats a not-guilty-by-reason-of-insanity verdict as an ordinary acquittal for liberty purposes; nearly all jurisdictions provide for automatic commitment proceedings, not release. (b) fabricates a sentence-based cap; commitment duration is tied to clinical status and can be effectively indefinite in many jurisdictions. (c) falsely universalizes the psychiatric-security-review-board model; only a small number of states (notably Oregon and Connecticut) use psychiatric security review boards with direct release authority, and in most jurisdictions, judges retain final release authority. (e) fabricates a *Kahler* holding on jury instructions; *Kahler* addressed only whether states may abolish the moral-wrongness insanity defense, not the use of instructions about commitment consequences.
